-142.053 (SAPPHO, Fragments)
SAPPHO, Fragments
	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]
	[b]Sappho[/b]

	1 D. H. [c dimgray]Comp.[/c] 23 (vi 114ss. Usener–Radermacher) (+P. Oxy. 2288)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	θήσω δὲ καὶ ταύτης παραδείγματα τῆς ἁρμονίας (sc. τῆς γλαφυρᾶς καὶ ἀνθηρᾶς συνθέσεως), ποιητῶν
	μὲν προχειρισάμενος Σαπφώ, ῥητόρων δὲ Ἰσοκράτην. ἄρξομαι δὲ ἀπὸ τῆς μελοποιοῦ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ποικιλόθρον᾿ ἀθανάτ᾿ Αφρόδιτα,
	παῖ Δίος δολόπλοκε, λίσσομαί σε,
	μή μ᾿ ἄσαισι μηδ᾿ ὀνίαισι δάμνα,
	[c dimgray]4[/c]πότνια, θῦμον,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀλλὰ τυίδ᾿ ἔλθ᾿, αἴ ποτα κἀτέρωτα
	τὰς ἔμας αὔδας ἀίοισα πήλοι
	ἔκλυες, πάτρος δὲ δόμον λίποισα
	[c dimgray]8[/c]χρύσιον ἦλθες
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 52[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	[b]Sappho[/b]
	Frr. 1–117 are arranged by the book of Sappho: 1–42 from Book 1, 43–52
	from Book 2, 53–57 from Book 3, 58–91 from Book 4, 92–101 from Book 5, 102
	from Book 7, 103 from Book 8, 104–117 from the Epithalamia. Frr. 118–168 cannot
	be assigned to any book and are arranged alphabetically. 169–192 are isolated words
	arranged alphabetically, 193–213 give information about the content of various passages
	of S.’s poetry.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	1 Dionysius of Halicarnassus, [c dimgray]On Literary Composition[/c]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	I shall give illustrations of this style (i.e. polished and exuberant composition), selecting
	Sappho among poets and Isocrates among orators, and I shall begin with the lyric poet:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Ornate-throned immortal Aphrodite, wile-weaving daughter of Zeus, I entreat you: do not
	overpower my heart, mistress, with ache and anguish, but come here, if ever in the past you
	heard my voice from afar and acquiesced and came, leaving your father’s golden house, with
	chariot yoked: beautiful swift sparrows
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]A papyrus fragment of early 2nd c. a.d. gives scraps of verses
	1–21. Since Hephaestion uses the poem to illustrate the Sapphic stanza, it was probably
	the first poem of Book 1.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 53[/c]

SAPPHO, Fragments

	ἄρμ᾿ ὐπασδεύξαισα· κάλοι δέ σ᾿ ἆγον
	ὤκεες στροῦθοι περὶ γᾶς μελαίνας
	πύκνα δίννεντες πτέρ᾿ ἀπ᾿ ὠράνωἴθε-
	[c dimgray]12[/c]ρος διὰ μέσσω,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	αἶψα δ᾿ ἐξίκοντο· σὺ δ᾿, ὦ μάκαιρα,
	μειδιαίσαισ᾿ ἀθανάτῳ προσώπῳ
	ἤρε᾿ ὄττι δηὖτε πέπονθα κὤττι
	[c dimgray]16[/c]δηὖτε κάλημμι,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	κὤττι μοι μάλιστα θέλω γένεσθαι
	μαινόλᾳ θύμῳ· τίνα δηὖτε πείθω
	ἄψ σ᾿ ἄγην ἐς ϝὰν φιλότατα; τίς σ᾿, ὦ
	[c dimgray]20[/c]Ψάπφ᾿, ἀδικήει;
	[m1]\ [/m]

	καὶ γὰρ αἰ φεύγει, ταχέως διώξει·
	αἰ δὲ δῶρα μὴ δέκετ᾿, ἀλλὰ δώσει·
	αἰ δὲ μὴ φίλει, ταχέως φιλήσει
	[c dimgray]24[/c]κωὐκ ἐθέλοισα.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἔλθε μοι καὶ νῦν, χαλέπαν δὲ λῦσον
	ἐκ μερίμναν, ὄσσα δέ μοι τέλεσσαι
	θῦμος ἰμέρρει, τέλεσον· σὺ δ᾿ αὔτα
	[c dimgray]28[/c]σύμμαχος ἔσσο.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ταύτης τῆς λέξεως ἡ εὐέπεια καὶ ἡ χάρις ἐν τῇ συνεχείᾳ καὶ λειότητι
	γέγονε τῶν ἁρμονιῶν. παρακεῖται γὰρ ἀλλήλοις τὰ ὀνόματα καὶ συνύφανται κατά τινας οἰκειότητας
	καὶ συζυγίας φυσικὰς τῶν γραμμάτων . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	cf. D. H. epitom. 23 (vi 185ss. U.–R.), Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 14. 1, schol. A et Choerob.
	in Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 11 et 14 (pp. 43s., 146, 244, 249ss. Consbr.), Hdn. 2. 948 Lentz, [c dimgray]Et.
	Gen.[/c] p. 31 Calame, [c dimgray]Et. Gud.[/c] 294. 37ss., Prisc. [c dimgray]Inst. Gr.[/c] 1. 37, Hsch. K 1683,
	Ω 112, Athen. 9. 391f, Ap. Dysc. [c dimgray]Adv.[/c] 1. 197 Schn.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	1 -όθρον᾿, -οφρον (vel sim.) codd.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	19 .\]ψ σ. ἄγην\[ pap. μαισαγηνεσσαν vel και- codd.
	ϝὰν Edmonds
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 54[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	whirring fast-beating wings brought you
	above the dark earth down from heaven through the mid-air, and soon they arrived; and you,
	blessed one, with a smile on your immortal face asked what was the matter with me this time and
	why I was calling this time and what in my maddened heart I most wished to happen for myself:
	‘Whom am I to persuade this time to lead you back to her love? Who wrongs you, Sappho? If she
	runs away, soon she shall pursue; if she does not accept gifts, why, she shall give them
	instead; and if she does not love, soon she shall love even against her will.’ Come to me now
	again and deliver me from oppressive anxieties; fulfil all that my heart longs to fulfil, and
	you yourself be my fellow-fighter.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The euphony and charm of this passage lie in the cohesion and smoothness of the joinery. Words
	are juxtaposed and interwoven according to certain natural affinities and groupings of the
	letters . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 55[/c]

	ss.)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	δεῦρύ μ᾿ ἐκ Κρήτας ἐπ\[ὶ τόνδ\]ε ναῦον
	ἄγνον, ὄππ\[ᾳ τοι\] χάριεν μὲν ἄλσος
	μαλί\[αν\], βῶμοι δὲ τεθυμιάμε-
	[c dimgray]4[/c]νοι \[λι\]βανώτῳ·
	ἐν δ᾿ ὔδωρ ψῦχρον κελάδει δι᾿ ὔσδων
	μαλίνων, βρόδοισι δὲ παῖς ὀ χῶρος
	ἐσκίαστ᾿, αἰθυσσομένων δὲ φύλλων
	[c dimgray]8[/c]κῶμα κατέρρει·
	ἐν δὲ λείμων ἰππόβοτος τέθαλεν
	ἠρίνοισιν ἄνθεσιν, αἰ δ᾿ ἄηται
	μέλλιχα πνέοισιν \[
	[c dimgray]12[/c]\[  \]
	ἔνθα δὴ σὺ . . . . έλοισα Κύπρι
	χρυσίαισιν ἐν κυλίκεσσιν ἄβρως
	ὀμμεμείχμενον θαλίαισι νέκταρ
	[c dimgray]16[/c]οἰνοχόαισον
	[m1]\ [/m]
	1 δευρυμεκρητα. \[ \].
	ναυγον ostr., interp. et suppl. Galiano, Lobel
	2 suppl. Page
	3 δεμιθυμ- ostr. δὲ τεθυμ- Norsa
	5 υσχων ostr.
	9 Sitzler: καταιριον ostr. καταρρεῖ Hermog.
	10 Vogliano: ττριννοισ ostr. Page: αιααιηται ostr.
	13 συ . . μελοισα vel συ . . ανελοισα ostr.
	14 ακρως ostr. ἀβροῖς Athen.
	15 Gallavotti: εμμειχ- vel ἀμμειχ- ostr. συνμεμίγμενον Athen.
	Hermog. [c dimgray]Id.[/c] 2. 4 (p. 331 Rabe)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	καὶ (sc. τῶν ἡδονῶν) τὰς μὲν οὐκ αἰσχρὰς ἔστιν ἁπλῶς ἐκφράζειν, οἷον κάλλος χωρίου καὶ φυτείας
	διαφόρους καὶ ῥευμάτων ποικιλίας καὶ ὅσα τοιαῦτα· ταῦτα γὰρ καὶ τῇ ὄψει προσβάλλει ἡδονὴν
	ὁρώμενα καὶ τῇ ἀκοῇ, ὅτε ἐξαγγέλλει τις, ὥσπερ ἡ Σαπφὼ ῾ἀμφὶ δὲ ὕδωρ ψυχρὸν κελαδεῖ δι᾿ ὔσδων
	μαλίνων᾿ καὶ ῾αἰθυσσομένων δὲ φύλλων κῶμα καταρρεῖ᾿ καὶ ὅσα πρὸ τούτων τε καὶ μετὰ ταῦτα
	εἴρηται.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 56[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	2 Potsherd of the 3rd c. b.c.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Hither
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] to me from Crete to this holy temple, where is your delightful grove of
	apple-trees, and altars smoking with incense; therein cold water babbles through apple-branches,
	and the whole place is shadowed by roses, and from the shimmering leaves the sleep of
	enchantment comes down; therein too a meadow, where horses graze, blossoms with spring flowers,
	and the winds blow gently . . .; there, Cypris, take . . . and pour gracefully into golden cups
	nectar that is mingled with our festivities.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Hermogenes, [c dimgray]Kinds of Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	It is possible to describe in simple terms pleasures which are not base, the beauty of a
	place, for example, the variety of plant-life, the diversity of streams and so on. These things
	afford pleasure to the eye when seen and to the ear when spoken about. Compare Sappho: ‘And
	round about cold water babbles through apple-branches’ and ‘from the shimmering leaves the sleep
	of enchantment flows down’ and all that comes before and after this.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The poem did not necessarily begin here: before δεῦρύ μ᾿ the potsherd has
	νοθενκατιου\[, ‘coming down from heaven(?), from the mountain
	top(?)’.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 57[/c]

	5 3rd c. papyrus
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(Cypris and) Nereids, grant that my brother arrive here unharmed and that everything he wishes
	in his heart be fulfilled, and grant too that he atone for all his past mistakes and be a joy to
	his friends and a bane to his enemies, and may no one ever again be a grief to us; grant that he
	may be willing to bring honour to his sister . . . grievous sufferings . . . formerly sorrowing
	. . . hearing . . . millet-seed . . . (accusation?) of the citizens . . . and do you,
	[m1]\ [/m]

-142.065 (SAPPHO, Fragments)
%Greek Lyric Sappho
SAPPHO, Fragments
	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]

	9 P. Oxy. 2289 fr. 4
	[m1]\ [/m]

	16 Same papyrus +
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Some say a host of cavalry, others of infantry, and others of ships, is the most beautiful
	thing on the black earth, but I say it is whatsoever a person loves. It is perfectly easy to
	make this understood by everyone: for she who far surpassed mankind in beauty, Helen, left her
	most noble husband and went sailing off to Troy with no thought at all for her child or dear
	parents, but (love) led her astray . . . lightly . . . (and she?) has reminded me now of
	Anactoria who is not here; I would rather see her lovely walk and the bright sparkle of her face
	than the Lydians’ chariots and armed infantry
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] . . . impossible to happen . . .
	mankind . . . but to pray to share . . . unexpectedly.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Perhaps the poem ended here, in which case the next three stanzas formed a
	complete poem. Helen is the theme of Alc. 42, 283.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 67[/c]

	17 Same papyrus +
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Let your (graceful form appear) near me (while I pray), lady Hera, to whom the Atridae,
	(illustrious) kings, made prayer(?); after accomplishing (many labours), first around (Ilium,
	then on the sea), they started out to this island,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] but could not (complete their
	journey) until they (called on) you and Zeus the god of suppliants and Thy one’s lovely
	(son);
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] now (be gracious and help me) in accordance with that ancient precedent.
	Holy and fair . . . maiden(s) . . . to be . . . to reach (the shrine?).
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	18 Same papyrus
	[m1]\ [/m]
	All . . . would . . . to say . . . (my) tongue . . . to tell stories . . . a man . . . greater
	. . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]

	ff.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Dionysus: for the shrine of the three gods cf. Alc. 129.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]Five stanzas in all. S. may have prayed to Hera for a safe voyage for herself or
	another. See also test. 59.
	[m1]\ [/m]

%Greek Lyric Sappho

	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]

	26 P. Oxy. 1231 fr. 16
	[m1]\ [/m]

	\]θαμέω\[
	ὄ\]ττινα\[ς γὰρ
	εὖ θέω, κῆνοί με μά\]ιστα πά\[ντων
	[c dimgray]4[/c]
	σίνοντα\]
	\]ἀλεμάτ᾿
	[c dimgray]11[/c]
	\]αν, ἔγω δ᾿ ἔμ᾿ \[αὔται
	[c dimgray]12[/c]τοῦτο σύ\]νοιδα
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf.  (p. 30 Calame) =  449. 37ss. . . . θέω, ὡς παρὰ Σαπφοῖ,
	οἷον· ὅττινας γὰρ εὖ θέω κεῖνοί με μάλιστα σίνονται, Ap. Dysc.  103a (i 80
	Schneider) = Alc. 317 καὶ παρὰ τοῖς Αἰολικοῖς δὲ ὡς ἐν παραθέσει ἀνεγνώσθη· ἔμ᾿ αὔτᾳ τοῦτ᾿ ἔγων

	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]

	31 ‘Longinus’, [c dimgray]On sublimity[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho, for example, always chooses the emotions associated with love’s madness from the
	attendant circumstances and the real situation. Where does she display her excellence? In that
	she is adept at selecting and combining the most important and excessive concomitants:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	He seems as fortunate as the gods to me, the man who sits opposite you and listens nearby to
	your sweet voice and lovely laughter. Truly that sets my heart trembling in my breast. For when
	I look at you for a moment, then it is no longer possible
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]End of the last poem in Book 1, which had 1320 lines, i.e. 330 Sapphic stanzas, perhaps 60–70 poems.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]The nightingale: cf. Hes. fr. 312 M.–W.

	[c dimgray]16[/c]φαίνομ᾿ ἔμ᾿ αὔ\[ᾳ.
	ἀλλὰ πὰν τόλματον, ἐπεὶ †καὶ πένητα†
	[m1]\ [/m]
	οὐ θαυμάζεις ὡς ὑπ<ὸ τὸ> αὐτὸ τὴν
	ψυχὴν τὸ σῶμα, τὰς ἀκοὰς τὴν γλῶσσαν, τὰς ὄψεις τὴν χρόαν, πάνθ᾿ ὡς ἀλλότρια διοιχόμενα
	ἐπιζητεῖ, καὶ καθ᾿ ὑπεναντιώσεις ἅμα ψύχεται καίεται, ἀλογιστεῖ φρονεῖ † ἢ γὰρ † φοβεῖται † ἢ
	παρ᾿ ὀλίγον τέθνηκεν ἵνα μὴ ἕν τι περὶ αὐτὴν πάθος φαίνηται, παθῶν δὲ σύνοδος; πάντα μὲν τοιαῦτα
	γίνεται περὶ τοὺς ἐρῶντας, ἡ λῆψις δ᾿ ὡς ἔφην τῶν ἄκρων καὶ ἡ εἰς ταὐτὸ συναίρεσις ἀπειργάσατο
	τὴν ἐξοχήν.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	15 Hermann: πιδεύσην cod. P

	32 Ap. Dysc. [c dimgray]Pron.[/c] 144a (i 113 Schneider)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Αἰολεῖς ἀμμέτερον καὶ ἄμμον καὶ ὔμμον καὶ σφόν. Σαπφώ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	αἴ με τιμίαν ἐπόησαν ἔργα
	τὰ σφὰ δοῖσαι
	1 Bergk: εμετιμιαν cod. A
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 80[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	for me to speak; my
	tongue has snapped,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] at once a subtle fire has stolen beneath my flesh, I see
	nothing with my eyes, my ears hum, sweat pours from me, a trembling seizes me all over, I am
	greener than grass, and it seems to me that I am little short of dying. But all can be endured,
	since . . . even a poor man . . .
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Are you not amazed how at one and the same moment she seeks out soul, body, hearing, tongue,
	sight, complexion as though they had all left her and were external, and how in contradiction
	she both freezes
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] and burns, is irrational and sane, is afraid and nearly dead, so
	that we observe in her not one single emotion but a concourse of emotions? All this of course
	happens to people in love; but, as I said, it is her selection of the most important details
	and her combination of them into a single whole that have produced the excellence of the
	poem.
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	32 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Pronouns[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Aeolic has the forms ἀμμέτερος and ἄμμος, ‘our’, ὔμμος, ‘your’, and σφός, ‘their’; cf.
	Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . who
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] made me honoured by the gift of their works
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. Lucr. 3. 155 [c dimgray]infringi linguam[/c].
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Catullus 51 is an adaptation of S.’s poem: see G. Wills, [c dimgray]G.R.B.S.[/c] 8
	(1967) 167 ff.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]‘Longinus’ must have read ψῦχρος, ‘cold’, in v. 13.
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c]See also fr. 213B (Voigt).
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The Muses? Cf. 193.

	33 Apollonius Dyscolus, Syntax
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . there are the adverbs that indicate prayer (e.g. αἴθε, ‘if only’):
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Golden-crowned Aphrodite, if only I could obtain this lot.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	34 Eustathius on [c dimgray]Iliad[/c] 8. 555
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Note that in the expression ‘around the shining moon’ one should not understand the light of
	a full moon; for then the stars are dim because they are outshone, as Sappho says
	somewhere:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The stars hide away their shining form around the lovely moon when in all her fullness she
	shines (over all) the earth.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho . . . says the moon is silver and because of this conceals the other stars from
	view.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	35 Strabo, [c dimgray]Geography[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	If Homer, having spoken of the Phoenicians, also calls
	them Sidonians after their mother-city Sidon, he is only using a common figure of speech, as for
	example . . . ‘and he came to Ida, . . . to Gargarus’ . . . and Sappho’s line:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	either Cyprus or Paphos
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] or Panormus (detains) you
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]S. probably went on to speak of a girl who outshone her companions in beauty: see
	Julian’s allusion and cf. 96. 6 ff. Julian, [c dimgray]Letters[/c]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Paphos is a city of Cyprus; Panormus is probably the Sicilian city (modern Palermo). See test. 47.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	36
	[c dimgray]Elymologicum Genuinum[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The Aeolic writers . . . use ποθήω for ποθέω, ‘I long’, e.g.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	and I long and yearn

	37
	[c dimgray]Etymologicum Genuinum[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And the Aeolic writers call pain σταλαγμός, ‘a dripping’; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	in my pain,
	for they (pains or wounds?) drip and flow.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray]Etymologicum Genuinum[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	For in Aeolic σσ is changed to ζ. ἐπιπλήσσω becomes ἐπιπλάζω; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	May winds and sorrows carry off the one who rebukes me.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	you roast
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] us,
	Sappho in Book 1.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	39 Scholiast on Aristophanes, [c dimgray]Peace[/c]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	and a gay leather strap
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	covered (her?) feet, a fine piece of Lydian work.

%Greek Lyric Sappho

	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]

	σοί, ‘to you’, the Attic form, is used also in Ionic and Aeolic; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	and to you I . . . of a white goat
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]

	41 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Pronouns[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The Aeolic form is ὔμμιν, ‘to you’:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Towards you lovely ones my thoughts are unchangeable.

	44 Same papyrus
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . Cyprus . . . the herald came (running . . ., and when he had stopped spoke) these words,
	Idaeus,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] the swift messenger and of the rest of Asia . . . undying fame. Hector and
	his companions are bringing the lively-eyed, graceful Andromache from holy Thebe and
	ever-flowing Placia in their ships over the salt sea; and (there are) many golden bracelets and
	(perfumed?) purple robes, ornate trinkets and countless silver drinking-cups and ivory.’ So he
	spoke; and nimbly his dear father leapt up, and the news went to his friends throughout the
	spacious city. At once the sons of Ilus yoked the mules to the smooth-running carriages, and the
	whole crowd of women and (tender?-) ankled maidens climbed on board. Apart (drove) the daughters
	of Priam . . . and unmarried men yoked horses to chariots, . . . and greatly . . . charioteers .
	. . (gap of several verses) . . . like gods
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] . . . holy . . . all together . . . set
	out . . . to Ilium, and the sweet-sounding pipe and cithara were mingled and the sound of
	castanets, and maidens sang clearly a holy song, and a marvellous echo reached the sky . . . and
	everywhere in the streets was . . . bowls and cups . . . myrrh and cassia
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]S.’s authorship is confirmed by quotations in Athenaeus, Bekker’s [c dimgray]Anecdota
	Graeca[/c] and Ammonius.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Trojan herald ([c dimgray]Il.[/c] 7. 248 ff., 24. 325 ff.).
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]Of Hector and Andromache.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	lovely high-pitched strain calling on Paean,

	[c dimgray][sup]5[/sup][/c]

	44A (= Alc 304 L.–P.)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Papyrus fragment written in 2nd or 3rd c. a.d., published [c dimgray]C.Q.[/c] 2 (1952)
	1–3 by Lobel and Page; assigned hesitantly by them to Alcaeus, but Treu ([c dimgray]Sappho[/c]
	161–4) argues for S.’s authorship: cf. Kirkwood, [c dimgray]E. G. M.[/c] 145–7.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(a) . . . (golden-haired Phoebus), whom the daughter
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] of Coeus bore, having lain
	with Cronus’ son, (god of the high clouds,) whose name is great; but Artemis swore the (gods’)
	great oath: ‘By your head, I shall always be a virgin (unwed), (hunting) on the peaks of the
	(lonely) mountains: come, grant this for my sake.’ So she spoke, and the father of the blessed
	gods nodded his consent; and gods (and men) call her (the virgin, shooter of deer,) huntress, a
	great title. Love, (loosener of limbs,) never approaches her . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	45 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Pronouns[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ὑμεῖς, ‘you’, is ὔμμες in Aeolic:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	as long as you wish,
	Sappho in Book 2.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	46 Herodian, [c dimgray]On Anomalous Words[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Let us return to the matter under discussion, namely τύλη, ‘cushion’, which was not used by
	Attic writers but is used by Sappho in Book 2:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	and I will lay down my limbs on soft cushions.

	Note that the τε is not a conjunction.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	47 Maximus of Tyre, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Socrates says Eros is a sophist, Sappho calls him a weaver of tales. Socrates is driven mad
	for Phaedrus by Eros, while Sappho’s heart is shaken by Eros like a wind falling on oaks on a
	mountain; (i.e.)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Love shook my heart like a wind falling on oaks on a mountain.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 93[/c]

	49 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook of Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Among the acatalectic types (of Aeolic dactylic verse) the pentameter is called the Sapphic
	fourteen-syllable, in which the whole of Sappho Book 2 is written; cf.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	I loved you, Atthis, once long ago.
	Plutarch, [c dimgray]Dialogue on Love[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Addressing a girl who was still too young for marriage Sappho says,
	[m1]\ [/m]
	You seemed to me a small, graceless child.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Julian begins, ‘You came: yes, you did; for thanks to your letter you came even
	although you were absent; and I . . .’
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The version of Terentianus Maurus suggests that the lines are consecutive, however unlikely that may seem.

	καὶ Σαπφώ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ψαύην δ᾿ οὐ δοκίμωμ᾿ ὀράνω †δυσπαχέα†
	 Ahrens: δοκεῖ μοι codd. Seidler: ὠρανῷ codd. δύσι πάχεσιν Bergk

	53 Schol. Theocr. 28 arg. (p. 334 Wendel)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	γέγραπται δὲ Αἰολίδι διαλέκτῳ παρὰ τὸ Σαπφικὸν ἐκκαιδεκασύλλαβον τὸ
	[m1]\ [/m]

	βροδοπάχεες ἄγναι Χάριτες δεῦτε Δίος κόραι
	 cf. Eust.  1429. 54ss., Philostr.  51, Heph.  10. 6 (p. 34 Consbr.) (Σαπφικὸν ἑκκαιδεκασύλλαβον, ᾧ τὸ τρίτον ὅλον Σαπφοῦς γέγραπται)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 96[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	50 Galen, [c dimgray]Exhortation to Learning[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Therefore, since we know that the prime of youth is like the spring flowers and brings
	short-lived enjoyment, it is better to commend the woman of Lesbos when she says:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	for he that is beautiful is beautiful as far as appearances go, while he that is good will
	consequently also be beautiful.

	51 Chrysippus, [c dimgray]Negatives[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . Sappho declaring:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	I do not know what I am to do; I am in two minds.

	52 Herodian, [c dimgray]On Anomalous Words[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho (uses the form ὄρανος, ‘sky’):
	[m1]\ [/m]

	I do not expect to touch the sky (with my two arms?)

	53 Argument to Theocritus 28
	[m1]\ [/m]
	It is written in the Aeolic dialect in the Sapphic sixteen-syllable metre; cf.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Hither, holy rosy-armed Graces, daughters of Zeus
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Probably the beginning of the first poem of Book 3.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 97[/c]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἔσσετ᾿ οὐδὲ πόθα εἰς ὔστερον· οὐ γὰρ πεδέχῃς
	βρόδων
	τὼν ἐκ Πιερίας, ἀλλ᾿ ἀφάνης κἀν Ἀίδα δόμῳ

	οὐδέποκ᾿ ὔ. codd.

	56 Chrysipp. π. ἀποφ. 13 ([c dimgray]S.V.F.[/c] ii 55 Arnim)

	οὐδ᾿ ἴαν δοκίμωμι προσίδοισαν φάος ἀλίω
	ἔσσεσθαι σοφίαν πάρθενον εἰς οὐδένα πω χρόνον

	54 Pollux, [c dimgray]Vocabulary[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	They say that Sappho was the first to use the word χλαμύς, ‘mantle’, when she said of
	Eros:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	. . . who had come from heaven clad in a purple mantle

	55 Stobaeus, [c dimgray]Anthology[/c] (on folly)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho to an uneducated woman
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	But when you die you will lie there, and afterwards there will never be any recollection of
	you or any longing for you since you have no share in the roses of Pieria
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]; unseen in
	the house of Hades also, flown from our midst, you will go to and fro among the shadowy
	corpses.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	56 Chrysippus, [c dimgray]Negatives[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . Sappho declared:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	I do not imagine that any girl who has looked on the light of the sun will have such
	skill
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] at any time in the future.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Plutarch says the lines were addressed ‘to a wealthy woman’, elsewhere ‘to an
	uncultured, ignorant woman’. See also frr. 147, 193.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]In Macedonia; birthplace of the Muses.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Probably poetic skill.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 99[/c]

	57 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho derides Andromeda
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] thus:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And what country girl beguiles your mind . . . dressed in country garb
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] . . . not
	knowing how to pull her rags over her ankles?
	[m1]\ [/m]

	58 3rd c. papyrus
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . (fleeing?) . . . (was bitten?) . . . (you of the many names?) . . . gives success to the
	mouth . . . fair gifts (of the deep-bosomed Muses?) . . . children . . . song-lover, (player) of
	clear-sounding lyres . . . old age already (withers?) all (my)
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] skin, and (my) hair
	(turned white) from black . . . (my) knees do not carry (me) . . . (to dance) like young fawns .
	. . but what could I do? . . . not possible to become (ageless?) . . . rosy-armed Dawn . . .
	carrying
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] (to) the ends of the earth . . . yet (age) seized (him) . . . (immortal?)
	wife . . . thinks . . . might give . . . but I love delicacy . . . love has obtained for me the
	brightness and beauty of the sun.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 omnia suppl. Hunt praeter
	9 Lobel,
	20 Lobel,
	22 Stiebitz
	9 πολνώ\]νυμον? Voigt
	11 Μοίσαν βαθυκόλ\]πων Stiebitz
	16 ὄρχ\]ησθ᾿ Edmonds
	25–26 ex Athen. 687b (= Clearch. fr. 41 Wehrli)

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. test 20.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]These lines are unmetrical.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Possibly ‘your’ or ‘her’.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Probably of Dawn carrying off Tithonus, to whom she gave eternal life but not
	eternal youth.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]End of poem; meaning of last lines uncertain: perhaps ‘love has kept me
	alive’.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	But Sappho expresses more simply the reason for our practice of wearing garlands when she
	says:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . reject . . . (as quickly as possible)
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] . . . and you, Dica, put lovely
	garlands around your locks, binding together stems of anise with your soft hands; for the
	blessed Graces look rather on what is adorned with flowers and turn away from the
	un-garlanded.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	She urges those offering sacrifice to garland themselves since what is more adorned with
	flowers is more pleasing to the gods.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	used it; e.g.

	94 Same parchment
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . and honestly I wish I were dead. She was leaving me with many tears and said
	this
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]: ‘Oh what bad luck has been ours, Sappho; truly I leave you against my will.’
	I replied to her thus: ‘Go and fare well and remember me, for you know how we eared for you. If
	not, why then I want to remind you . . . and the good times we had. You put on many wreaths of
	violets and roses and (crocuses?) together by my side, and round your tender neck you put many
	woven garlands made from flowers and . . . with much flowery perfume, fit for a queen, you
	anointed yourself . . . and on soft beds . . . you would satisfy your longing (for?) tender . .
	.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Or ‘often she said this too’ or again ‘she said much and this in particular’;
	‘and honestly I wish I were dead’ might be the words of the departing girl, not of
	Sappho.

	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	entered . . . I said, ‘Lord, . . . for by the blessed (goddess) . . . I get no pleasure from
	being above the earth, and a longing grips me to die and see the dewy, lotus-covered banks of
	Acheron . . .’
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. 22, 213, test. 2.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]S. seems to tell of a divine epiphany as e.g. in 1; Hermes guided the souls of
	the dead to the underworld.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 119[/c]

	96 Same parchment
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . Sardis . . . often turning her thoughts in this direction . . . (she honoured)
	you
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] as being like a goddess for all to see and took most delight in your song. Now
	she stands out among Lydian women like the rosy-fingered moon after sunset, surpassing all the
	stars, and its light spreads alike over the salt sea and the flowery fields; the dew is shed in
	beauty, and roses bloom and tender chervil and flowery melilot. Often as she goes to and fro
	she remembers gentle Atthis and doubtless her tender heart is consumed because of your fate . .
	. to go there . . . this . . . mind . . . much . . . sings . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Presumably Atthis; but S. may have written, ‘(she honoured) you as being like a
	goddess, Arignota, and took . . .’
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	(b) But for you, Cleis,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] I have no way of obtaining a decorated headband; but . .
	. the Mytilenean . . . to have . . . if . . . decorated . . . (the city has?) these memorials
	of the exile of the sons of Cleanax
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]; for these (of ours?) . . . wasted away
	dreadfully . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	99 3rd c. a.d. papyrus
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(a) . . . after a short (time?) . . . descendants of Polyanax
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] . . . strike the
	strings . . . receiving the ‘olisbos’
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] . . . kindly . . . quivers . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(b) Son of (Leto) and Zeus
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c] . . . come to your rites(?) . . . leaving woody
	(Gryneia?)
	[c dimgray][sup]5[/sup][/c] . . . oracle . . . sing . . . sister
	[c dimgray][sup]6[/sup][/c] . . . descendants of
	Polyanax
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] . . . I wish to expose the mad one . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]S.’s daughter: see testt. 1, 2.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]I.e. the family of Myrsilus: see Alc. test. 1, schol. Alc. 112. 23; for the exile
	of Myrsilus see Alc. 305(a) 18f. S. may be saying that luxuries (reminders of happy days in
	Mytilene) are not obtainable, now that she is in exile.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The lines may be by Alcaeus.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Cf. 155.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]‘Strings which welcome the plectrum’? (West) or ‘women who use the dildo’, perhaps with hostile reference to the descendants of Polyanax; text uncertain.
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c]Beginning of a poem addressed to Apollo; text very insecure,
	[c dimgray][sup]5[/sup][/c]In Asia Minor; for Apollo’s wooded shrine see Paus. 1. 21. 9.
	[c dimgray][sup]6[/sup][/c]Artemis, if the interpretation of the scraps is correct.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	100 Pollux, [c dimgray]Vocabulary[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	In Book 5 of Sappho’s lyric poems we may find:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	and covered her all over with soft shag,
	and they say this means pieces of (close-woven?) linen.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	101 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And when Sappho says in Book 5 of her lyric poems, addressing Aphrodite,
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . and
	handcloths . . . purple, perfumed (?), (which Mnasis) sent (to you) from Phocaea, expensive
	gifts . . .,
	she means handcloths as an adornment for the head; Hecataeus makes this clear
	. . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	101A (Voigt) = Alc. 347(b)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	102 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Common also is the antispastic tetrameter catalectic which has only its second unit
	antispastic,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] a metre in which Sappho wrote songs at the end of (or ‘in’) her
	seventh book:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Truly, sweet mother, I cannot weave my web, for I am overcome with desire for a boy because
	of slender Aphrodite.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The other units being iambic (⏑–⏑–/⏑– –
	⏑/⏑–⏑–/⏑– –); in modern
	analysis, iambic + glyconic + bacchius, or anacreontic.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 127[/c]

-142.131 (SAPPHO, Fragments)
%Greek Lyric Sappho
SAPPHO, Fragments
	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]

	104(a) Demetr. [c dimgray]Eloc.[/c] 141 (p. 33 Radermacher)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	104(a) Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sometimes, also, Sappho makes charming use of repetition as in the description of the Evening
	Star:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Hesperus, bringing everything that shining Dawn scattered, you bring the sheep, you
	bring the goat, you bring back the child to its mother.
	Here the charm lies in the repetition of the word ‘bring’, always with the same reference (i.e. to Hesperus).
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(b) Himerius, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	You are, I think, an evening star,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	the fairest of all the stars.
	This song to Hesperus is Sappho’s.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	105(a) Syrianus on Hermogenes, [c dimgray]On Kinds of Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And such kinds of style as describe what gives pleasure to the senses, sight, hearing, smell,
	taste, touch as . . . Sappho (fr. 2. 5–8) and
	[m1]\ [/m]
	As the sweet-apple reddens on the bough-top, on the top of the topmost bough; the
	apple-gatherers have forgotten it—no they have not forgotten it entirely, but they could
	not reach it.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 131[/c]

	ταὐτὸν ἀγαγεῖν τῷ ἥρωι τὸν νεανίσκον ταῖς πράξεσι.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(c) Demetr. [c dimgray]Eloc.[/c] 106 (p. 26 Radermacher)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τὸ δὲ ἐπιφώνημα καλούμενον ὁρίζοιτο μὲν ἄν τις λέξιν ἐπικοσμοῦσαν· ἔστι δὲ τὸ
	μεγαλοπρεπέστατον ἐν τοῖς λόγοις· τῆς γὰρ λέξεως ἡ μὲν ὑπηρετεῖ, ἡ δὲ ἐπικοσμεῖ. ὑπηρετεῖ μὲν ἡ
	τοιάδε· ῾οἴαν . . . καταστείβοισι᾿, ἐπικοσμεῖ δὲ τὸ ἐπιφερόμενον τό· ῾χάμαι . . . ἄνθος᾿.

	ἄνθος . . .

	106 Demetr. [c dimgray]Eloc.[/c] 146 (p. 34 Radermacher)

	 cf. Eust.  741. 16, Hsch. M1004

	107 Ap. Dysc. [c dimgray]Coni.[/c] 490 (i 223 Schneider)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἆρα· οὗτος κατὰ πᾶσαν διάλεκτον ὑπεσταλμένης τῆς κοινῆς καὶ Ἀττικῆς ἦρα λέγεται·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἦρ᾿ ἔτι παρθενίας ἐπιβάλλομαι; Σαπφώ.
	cf. schol. Vat. in Dion. Thrac. 20 (p. 290 Hilgard) π<αρ>θενίης Ap. Dysc.
	παρθενικὰς schol. Dion.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 132[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	(b) Himerius, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	It was Sappho who compared the girl to an apple . . . and likened the bridegroom to Achilles
	and put the young man on a par with the hero in his achievements.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(c) Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The so-called ‘epiphoneme’ might be defined as a phrase which adds ornamentation, and it is
	the most impressive feature in prose. Some phrases help the sense, others ornament it; one that
	helps the sense is ‘Like the hyacinth . . . mountains’; what follows ornaments it: ‘and on the
	ground . . . flower’. This addition to the foregoing words clearly adds ornament and
	beauty.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Like the hyacinth which shepherds tread underfoot in the mountains, and on the ground the
	purple flower . . .
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	106 Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Of the outstanding man Sappho says:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Superior, as the Lesbian singer to those of other lands
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]

	107 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Conjunctions[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	In every dialect except the Koine (Common) and Attic ἆρα has the form ἦρα; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Do I still long for virginity?

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Attributed to Sappho by Bergk.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The expression ‘after the Lesbian singer’ became proverbial.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	door-keeper at the wedding, using prosaic rather than poetic language.

	[m1]\ [/m]

	On high the roof—Hymenaeus!—raise up, you
	carpenters—Hymenaeus
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]! The bridegroom is coming, the equal of Ares, much
	larger than a large man.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]

	112 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The same poetess (Sappho) uses the 3½-foot choriambic with iambic close (–
	⏑ ⏑ – ⏑ – –):
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Happy bridegroom, your marriage has been fulfilled as you prayed, you have the girl for whom
	you prayed . . . Your
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] form is graceful, your eyes . . . gentle, and love streams
	over your beautiful face . . . Aphrodite has honoured you outstandingly.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	113 Dionysius of Halicarnassus, [c dimgray]On Literary Composition[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	For, bridegroom, there (was?) never another girl like this one
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]

	followed by recantation.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]The context in Choricius shows that these words are addressed to the

	[m1]\ [/m]
	αἱ δὲ ἀπὸ τῶν σχημάτων χάριτες δῆλαί εἰσιν καὶ πλεῖσται παρὰ Σαπφοῖ, οἷον ἐκ τῆς ἀναδιπλώσεως,
	ὅπου νύμφη πρὸς τὴν παρθενίαν φησί, ῾παρθενία . . . οἴχῃ᾿, ἡ δὲ ἀποκρίνεται πρὸς αὐτὴν τῷ αὐτῷ
	σχήματι, ῾οὐκέτι . . . ἥξω᾿.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	παρθενία, παρθενία, ποῖ με λίποισ᾿ ἀποίχῃ;
	† οὐκέτι ἤξω πρὸς σέ, οὐκέτι ἤξω †.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	1 Blomfield: λιποῦσα οἴχῃ cod.

	115 Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 7. 6 (p. 23 Consbruch)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	τίῳ σ᾿, ὦ φίλε γάμβρε, καλῶς ἐικάσδω;
	ὄρπακι βραδίνῳ σε μάλιστ᾿ ἐικάσδω.

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀντὶ τοῦ νυμφίε.

	117 Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 4.2 (p. 13 Consbruch)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	καταληκτικὰ δὲ (καλεῖται μέτρα), ὅσα μεμειωμένον ἔχει τὸν τελευταῖον πόδα, οἷον ἐπὶ ἰαμβικοῦ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	† χαίροις ἀ νύμφα †, χαιρέτω δ᾿ ὀ γάμβρος
	 cf. Choerob. in Heph. 4 (p. 220 Consbr.), Epit. Heph. 8 (p. 361 Consbr.), Theocr. 18. 49, Nic.
	Chon. (ed. Moravcsik,  12, 1964, 473ss.)
	ἀ νύμφα, χαίροις? Lobel. Sa. ded. Ursinus
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 138[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	114 Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The graces arising from the use of figures of speech are clear and numerous in Sappho; for
	example, her use of repetition where a bride addresses her virginity, and her virginity replies
	to her using the same figure:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	‘Virginity, virginity, where have you gone, deserting me?’ ‘Never again shall I come to you:
	never again shall I come.’
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	115 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And the Aeolic dactylic pentameters, catalectic with disyllabic close:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	To what may I well compare you, dear bridegroom? I compare you above all to a slender
	sapling.

	116 Servius on Virgil
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Many people say that [c dimgray]gener[/c] (‘son-in-law’) is used here for ‘husband’, just as in
	Sappho, who in the book entitled ‘Wedding-songs’ says:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Farewell, bride, farewell, worthy bridegroom,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	using γάμβρος for νυμφίος.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	117 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Metres are called catalectic when they have the final foot shortened, as in the iambic:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Farewell, bride, and farewell the bridegroom.

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Text and metre of v. 2 uncertain.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]γάμβρος is used of any male relation by marriage.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 139[/c]

-142.141 (SAPPHO, Fragments)

	117A (= 194A Voigt) Mich. Ital. [c dimgray]Or. ad Mich. Oxit.[/c] ([c dimgray]C.R.[/c] 10, 1960, 192s.,
	[c dimgray]Herm.[/c] 91, 1963, 115ss.)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	σοὶ τῷ καλλίστῳ νυμφίῳ καὶ καθαρωτάτῳ ἀνδρί, θειότατε ἀρχιερεῦ, ὡς ἐπὶ γάμοις μυστικοῖς
	ἐπιθαλάμιον ᾄδομεν, οὐχ οἷον . . . οὐδ᾿ οἷον ᾄδει Σαπφὼ ἡ ποιήτρια, μαλακοῖς τισι ῥυθμοῖς καὶ
	μέλεσιν ἐκλελυμένοις τὰς ᾠδὰς διαπλέκουσα καὶ ἵπποις μὲν ἀθλοφόροις ἀπεικάζουσα τοὺς νυμφίους,
	ῥόδων δ᾿ ἁβρότητι παραβάλλουσα τὰς νυμφευομένας παρθένους καὶ τὸ φθέγμα πηκτίδος ἐμμελέστερον
	ποιοῦσα . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	117A (Voigt) Hsch. Ξ 85
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ξοάνων προθύρων·
	ἐξεσμένων
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Sa. ξ. π. ded. Latte,  32 (1953) 35s.

	117B (Voigt) = inc. auct. 24
	[m1]\ [/m]

	118 Hermog. [c dimgray]Id.[/c] 2. 4 (p. 334 Rabe)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	καθόλου τὸ περιτιθέναι τοῖς ἀπροαιρέτοις προαιρετικόν τι γλυκύτητα ποιεῖ, ὥσπερ . . . καὶ ὅταν
	τὴν λύραν ἐρωτᾷ ἡ Σαπφὼ καὶ ὅταν αὐτὴ ἀποκρίνηται, οἷον·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἄγι δὴ χέλυ δῖα † μοι λέγε †
	φωνάεσσα † δὲ γίνεο †
	καὶ τὰ ἑξῆς.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. Eust.  9. 41s., Mich. Ital.  (v. Page,  p.
	155)
	1 ἄγε δὴ, ἄγε μοι, ἄγε codd. 1s. δῖα χέλυ φ. γένοιο Eust.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 140[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	117A Michael of Italy, [c dimgray]Address to Michael Oxites[/c]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	For you, handsome bridegroom, man of purity, most holy high priest, we sing a wedding-song as
	at your mystic marriage
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]; not such a song as . . . the poetess Sappho sings, weaving
	her songs from soft rhythms and licentious melodies, comparing the bridegrooms to prize-winning
	horses and likening the brides to the tenderness of roses, making her utterance more tuneful
	than the lyre,
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	117A (Voigt) Hesychius, [c dimgray]Lexicon[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	smooth doorway,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	i.e. polished.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	117B (Voigt) = ‘Sappho or Alcaeus’ 24.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	118 Hermogenes, [c dimgray]Kinds of Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	In general the attribution of deliberate choice to things incapable of it produces a sweet
	effect, as . . . when Sappho questions her lyre and the lyre answers her:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Come, divine lyre, speak to me and find yourself a voice,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	and the lines which follow.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Bishop of Constantinople, [c dimgray]c[/c]. 1143.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]With the church.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]Cf. frr. 118, 156, test. 61 n. 1.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Attributed to S.’s Epithalamia since Catullus’ [c dimgray]rasilem[/c] . . . [c dimgray]forem[/c],
	‘polished doorway’, occurs in a wedding-hymn (61. 161).
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Text uncertain.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 141[/c]

	119 Schol. Aristoph. [c dimgray]Plut.[/c] 729 (p. 364 Dübner)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἡμιτύβιον· ἀντὶ τοῦ σουδάριον· ῥάκος ἡμι τριβὲς λινοῦν τι, οἷον ἐκμαγεῖον. καὶ Σαπφώ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	αἰμιτύβιον στάλασσον
	Hemsterhuis: σταλάσσων cod.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	120
	[c dimgray]Et. Mag.[/c] 2. 43
	[m1]\ [/m]
	βάζω τὸ λέγω· . . . ἐξ αὐτοῦ γίνεται ἀβακής· κέχρηται δὲ αὐτῷ Σαπφώ, οἶον·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀλλά τις οὐκ ἔμμι παλιγκότων
	ὄργαν, ἀλλ᾿ ἀβάκην τὰν φρέν᾿ ἔχω . . .
	 cf. Zonar. in ἀβάκησαν
	2 Ursinus: ὀργάνων . . . πάμφρενα, παμφρένα codd.

	121 Stob. [c dimgray]Ecl.[/c] 4. 22. 112 (iv 543 Wachsmuth–Hense) (ὅτι ἐν τοῖς γάμοις
	τὰς τῶν συναπτομένων ἡλικίας χρὴ σκοπεῖν)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Σαπφοῦς·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀλλ᾿ ἔων φίλος ἄμμι
	λέχος ἄρνυσο νεώτερον·
	οὐ γὰρ τλάσομ᾿ ἔγω συνοί-
	κην ἔοισα γεραιτέρα
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. Arsen.
	3 14 = Apostol.
	2 52d (ii 277 Leutsch–Schn.) metrum incert.
	4 Hiller: ἔσσα codd.

	122 Athen. 12. 554b (iii 223 Kaibel)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	φυσικὸν γὰρ δή τι τὸ τοὺς οἰομένους εἶναι καλοὺς καὶ ὡραίους ἀνθολογεῖν. ὅθεν αἴ τε περὶ τὴν
	Περσεφόνην ἀνθολογεῖν λέγονται καὶ Σαπφώ φησιν ἰδεῖν
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἄνθε᾿ ἀμέργοισαν παῖδ᾿ † ἄγαν † ἀπάλαν.

	παῖδα τὰν Blomfield
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 142[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	119 Scholiast on Aristophanes, [c dimgray]Plutus[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἡμιτύβιον: for [c dimgray]sudarium[/c], a half-worn linen cloth, like a napkin: cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	a dripping napkin

	120
	[c dimgray]Etymologicum Magnum[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	βάζω, ‘I speak’ . . .; from it comes ἀβακής, ‘unspeaking, quiet, gentle’, which Sappho uses,
	e.g.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	But I am not one of those with a spiteful temperament: I have a gentle heart.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	121 Stobaeus, [c dimgray]Anthology[/c] (that in marriage the age of the partners should be
	taken into account)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	But if you are my friend, take the bed of a younger woman, for I will not endure being the
	elder one in a partnership.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	122 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	For it is natural that those who think themselves beautiful and ripe should gather flowers.
	This is why Persephone and her companions are said to gather flowers, and Sappho says she
	saw
	[m1]\ [/m]
	a tender girl picking flowers
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. Anacr. 416.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 143[/c]

-142.145 (SAPPHO, Fragments)

SAPPHO, Fragments

	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]

	123 Ammon. [c dimgray]Diff.[/c] 75 (p. 19 Nickau)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἄρτι καὶ ἀρτίως διαφέρει. ἄρτι μὲν γάρ ἐστι χρονικὸν ἐπίρρημα, τὸ δ᾿ ἀρτίως ἐπὶ τοῦ
	ἀπηρτισμένου ἔργου τελείως. ὥστε ἁμαρτάνει Σαπφὼ λέγουσα·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀρτίως μὲν ἀ χρυσοπέδιλος Αὔως

	ἀντὶ χρονικοῦ ἐπιρρήματος.
	μ᾿ ἀ Seidler ut Sapphica evaderet stropha
	[m1]\ [/m]

	124 Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 15. 4 (p. 48 Consbruch)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	δύναται δὲ καὶ εἰς τρίτον ἀνάπαιστον διαιρεῖσθαι, εἰ ἀπὸ σπονδείου ἄρχοιτο, οἷον τὸ Σαπφοῦς·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	αὐτὰ δὲ σὺ Καλλιόπα,

	τοῦ προσοδιακοῦ ὂν καὶ τοῦτο εἶδος.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. schol. A in Heph. (p. 154 Consbr.)

	125 Schol. Aristoph. [c dimgray]Thesm.[/c] 401 (p. 267 Dübner)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	νεωτέρων καὶ ἐρωτικῶν τὸ στεφανηπλοκεῖν· πρὸς τὸ ἔθος, ὅτι ἐστεφανηπλόκουν αἱ παλαιαί. Σαπφώ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	† αυταόρα † ἐστεφαναπλόκην
	αὔτα δ᾿ ὡράα Ahrens αὔτα|ὠράα στ- Treu αἰστεφανηπλόκουν cod.
	ἐστ- Seidler -απλόκην Ahrens
	[m1]\ [/m]

	126
	[c dimgray]Et. Gen.[/c] (p. 22 Calame) = [c dimgray]Et. Mag.[/c] 250. 10s.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	δαύω· τὸ κοιμῶμαι· Σαπφώ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	δαύοις ἀπάλας ἐτα<ί>ρας ἐν στήθεσιν.
	λέγει δὲ Ἡρωδιανὸς (i 453 Lentz) ὅτι ἅπαξ κεῖται ἡ λέξις παρὰ
	Σαπφοῖ.
	δαύοισ᾿? Bergk ἑτάρας ἐν, ἐτάρασσε(ν) codd. ἐταίρας|ἐν Ahrens
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 144[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	123 Ammonius, [c dimgray]On Similar but Different Words[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	There is a difference between ἄρτι and ἀρτίως; for ἄρτι is an adverb of time, while ἀρτίως is
	used of something which is finished completely. So Sappho is wrong when she says:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Golden-sandalled Dawn
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] had lately . . .
	using ἀρτίως instead of an adverb of time.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	124 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The length ⏓ – ⏑ ⏑ – ⏑ ⏑
	– can be divided to form three anapaests if it begins with a spondee, as in Sappho’s
	[m1]\ [/m]

	and you yourself, Calliope,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	which is also a type of prosodiac.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	125 Scholiast on Aristophanes, [c dimgray]Thesmophoriazusae[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	It was young folk and people in love who wove garlands; with reference to the custom by which
	women of old used to wear garlands; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	(I myself in my youth?) used to weave garlands.

	126
	[c dimgray]Etymologicum Genuinum[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	δαύω, ‘I sleep’; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	May you sleep
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] on the bosom of your tender companion.
	Herodian says that the word occurs once only in Sappho.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. 103. 13.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]One of the Muses.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Or ‘sleeping’, feminine participle.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 145[/c]

	δεῦτέ νυν ἄβραι Χάριτες καλλίκομοί τε Μοῖσαι

	γλυκύπικρον ἀμάχανον ὄρπετον

	1 Seidler: δαῦτε codd.

	127 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho has composed the line which contains two ithyphallics:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Hither again, Muses, leaving the golden (house of your father, Zeus?)

	128 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . and the choriambic tetrameter,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] which is found in longer sequences, as in
	Sappho’s lines which begin:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Hither now, tender Graces and lovely-haired Muses.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]

	129 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Pronouns[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἐμέθεν (‘of me’) is frequently used by the Aeolic poets:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	(a) and you have forgotten me
	(b) or you love some other (more?) than me

	130 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The Aeolic dactylic tetrameter acatalectic is as follows:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Once again limb-loosening Love makes me tremble, the bitter-sweet, irresistible
	creature.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Heph. notes that the line ends in an amphibrach or (as here) a
	bacchius.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Cf. 103. 8.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Perhaps followed immediately by 131.

	131 Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 7. 7 (v. ad fr. 130)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Ἄτθι, σοὶ δ᾿ ἔμεθεν μὲν ἀπήχθετο
	φροντίσδην, ἐπὶ δ᾿ Ἀνδρομέδαν πότῃ
	 cf. schol. B in Heph. 9 (p. 274 Consbr.)
	2 Bentley: φροντὶς δ᾿ ἦν codd. Voigt: πότη, πότε codd.

	132 Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 15. 18 (p. 53s. Consbruch)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἄλλο ἀσυνάρτητον ὁμοίως κατὰ τὴν πρώτην ἀντιπάθειαν, ἐκ τροχαϊκοῦ διμέτρου ἀκαταλήκτου καὶ
	ἰαμβικοῦ ἑφθημιμεροῦς, ὅπερ ἐὰν παραλλάξῃ τὴν τομήν, γίνεται τροχαϊκὸν προκαταληκτικον·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἔστι μοι κάλα πάις χρυσίοισιν ἀνθέμοισιν
	ἐμφέρη <ν> ἔχοισα μόρφαν Κλέις ἀγαπάτα,
	ἀντὶ τᾶς ἔγωὐδὲ Λυδίαν παῖσαν οὐδ᾿ ἐράνναν . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τούτων δὲ τὸ μὲν δεύτερον δῆλόν ἐστιν
	ἀπὸ τῆς τομῆς ὅτι οὕτως σύγκειται ὡς προείρηται, ἐκ τοῦ τροχαϊκοῦ διμέτρου ἀκαταλήκτου καὶ τοῦ
	ἑφθημιμεροῦς ἰαμβικοῦ, τὸ δὲ πρῶτον, διὰ τὸ πρὸ συλλαβῆς ἔχειν τὴν τομήν, ἐγένετο
	προκαταληκτικόν, ἐκ τροχαϊκοῦ ἑφθημιμεροῦς, ῾ἔστι μοι κάλα πάις᾿, καὶ διμέτρου ἀκαταλήκτου τοῦ
	῾χρυσίοισιν ἀνθέμοισιν᾿· τὸ δὲ τρίτον ἐξ ὑπερκαταλήκτου, ῾ἀντὶ τᾶς ἔγωὐδὲ Λυδίαν᾿ καὶ
	βραχυκαταλήκτου, ῾παῖσαν οὐδ᾿
	ἐράνναν᾿.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. schol. A in Heph. 15 (p. 159 Consbr.)
	2 post Κλέις fort. deest syllaba; ᾱγαπ- = ἀ ἀγαπ-

	133 Heph. [c dimgray]Ench.[/c] 14.7 (p. 46 Consbruch)

	τὸ τοιοῦτον, οἷον παρὰ
	Σαπφοῖ·

	131 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(But?), Atthis, the thought of me has grown hateful to you, and you fly off to
	Andromeda.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	132 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Similarly there is another ‘asynartete’ or ‘unconnected’ metre of the first type of
	opposition (i.e. trochaic clashing with iambic), composed of a trochaic dimeter acatalectic and
	an iambic 3½-foot length; if the caesura is transposed, the line becomes a procatalectic
	trochaic
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	I have a beautiful child who looks like golden
	flowers, my darling Cleis, for whom I would not (take)
	all Lydia or lovely
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] . . .
	The second line is shown clearly by the caesura to be composed, as I have said. of the
	trochaic dimeter acatalectic and the iambic 3½-foot length; the first line thanks to its early
	caesura has become procatalectic, formed from a trochaic 3½-foot length (ἔστι μοι κάλα πάις)
	and an acatalectic (trochaic) dimeter (χρυσίοισιν ἀνθέμοισιν); line 3 is composed of a
	hypercatalectic (trochaic dimeter)
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] (ἀντὶ τᾶς ἔγωὐδὲ Λυδίαν) and a brachycatalectic
	(trochaic dimeter)
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c] (παῖσαν οὐδ᾿ ἐράνναν).
	[m1]\ [/m]

	133 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	But when the ionic is anaclastic (i.e. has its syllables inverted: ⏑ ⏑
	– ⏑ – ⏑ – –), it is preceded by an iambic of
	six or seven short-units (i.e. ⏓ – ⏑ –) with the
	following result, as in Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]See on 130.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]I.e. with early catalexis as in v. 1 (tro. dim. cat. + tro. dim.);
	on the metrical problems of the fr. see Page, [c dimgray]S.[/c]
	& [c dimgray]A.[/c] 131 n. 4: our text of v. 2 does not suit Heph.’s analysis.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Perhaps ‘lovely Lesbos’.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]I.e. a dimeter with an extra final syllable.
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c]I.e. a dimeter with two syllables suppressed.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	εἰπόντος τοῦ Ἀλκαίου·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	θέλω τί τ᾿ εἴπην, ἀλλά με κωλύει
	αἴδως . . .
	. . .
	[c dimgray]3[/c]αἰ δ᾿ ἦχες ἔσλων ἴμερον ἢ κάλων
	καὶ μή τί τ᾿ εἴπην γλῶσσ᾿ ἐκύκα κάκον,

	Steph. schol. ad loc. (p. 280 Rabe) (=  i 266 Cramer) (εἴτε ὁ Ἀλκαῖος ὁ
	ποιητὴς ἤρα κόρης τινὸς ἢ ἄλλος τις ἤρα, παράγει οὖν ὅμως ἡ Σαπφὼ διάλογον· καὶ λέγει ὁ ἐρῶν
	πρὸς τὴν ἐρωμένην, θέλω κτλ., εἶτ᾿ αὖθις ἀμοιβαδὶς ἡ κόρη λέγει πρὸς ἐκεῖνον, ἀλλ᾿ ἐὰν ᾖς ἀγαθὸς
	καὶ ὃ ἔμελλες πρὸς μὲ εἰπεῖν ἧν ἀγαθόν, οὐκ ἂν ᾐδοῦ καὶ ᾐσχύνου οὕτως, ἀλλὰ μετὰ παρρησίας
	ἔλεγες ἂν βλέπων πρὸς μὲ ἀνερυθριάστως), tr. Lat. de Moerbeka (p. 211 Spengel), Ann. Comnen.
	15.9 (iii 223 Leib)
	5 κεν ὢς οὐκ Lee
	6 τὠδικαίως (= τούτω, τὸ ἐδικαίως) Lobel: cf. de Moer. sed hoc dicit (dicis
	cod. m) clamando de proprio iusto; ῾-αιο- Aeolicum non est’ (Voigt)

	136 Scholiast on Sophocles
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sophocles uses the expression ‘messenger of Zeus’ of the nightingale because it signals the
	coming of spring; . . . cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	the messenger of spring, the lovely-voiced nightingale

	137 Aristotle, [c dimgray]Rhetoric[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Men are ashamed to say, to do or to intend to do shameful things; cf. Sappho’s reply when
	Alcaeus said:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	‘I wish to say something to you, but shame prevents me.’ ‘. . . but if you had a desire for
	what is honourable or good, and your tongue were not stirring up something evil to say, shame
	would not cover your eyes, but you would state your claim.’
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	138 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And Sappho says to the man who is extravagantly admired for his figure and considered
	handsome:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Aristotle implies a poem by Alc. with an answering poem by S., and some edd.
	attach 1–2 to Alc. 384; but the anon, scholiast is probably correct to attribute
	1–2 like the rest to S., i.e. to credit her with a poem in which she reported the
	address and then answered it, and it is possible to take Aristotle’s words in this sense.
	Stephanus (11th or 12th c.) goes further: he says S. is writing a dialogue, and he throws
	doubt on the identity of both speakers. Perhaps S. wrote a poem in which the identity of the
	male speaker was unclear, and later biographers identified him falsely as Alc, noting that the
	rhythm was Alcaic (no other certain example in S.): see test. 8 for the type of error that a
	Peripatetic writer could make.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 153[/c]

	141 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Alcaeus (447) introduces Hermes too as the gods’ wine-pourer, as does Sappho, who
	says
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] (vv. 1–3):
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Presumably a dialogue between worshippers and Aphrodite; this is the earliest
	mention of the Adonis cult, which reached Asia Minor from Syria via Cyprus; see
	168.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Linus who met his doom (οἶτος).
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Paus. calls him the writer of the oldest Athenian hymns, but he may have been a Hellenistic poet.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]S.’s description of a divine wedding (e.g. that of Peleus and Thetis) may be from
	a wedding-song.

-142.161 (SAPPHO, Fragments)
%Greek Lyric Sappho

	148 Schol. Pind. [c dimgray]Ol.[/c] 2. 96f (i 85s. Drachmann)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ὁ νοῦς· ὁ δὲ πλοῦτος οὐ μόνος ὢν καθ᾿ ἑαυτόν, ἀλλὰ καὶ ἀρετῇ κεκοσμημένος, καιρίως τῶν τε
	ἑαυτοῦ ἀγαθῶν καὶ τῆς ἀρετῆς ἀπολαύει, συνετὴν ἔχων τὴν φροντίδα πρὸς τὸ ἀγρεύειν τὰ καλά.
	τούτων γὰρ τὸ ἕτερον καθ᾿ ἑαυτὸ οὐχ ἡδύ· ὡς καὶ Καλλίμαχος ([c dimgray]Jov.[/c] 95s.) καὶ ἡ Σαπφώ·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ὀ πλοῦτος ἄνευ † ἀρέτας οὐκ ἀσίνης πάροικος,
	ἀ δ᾿ ἀμφοτέρων κρᾶσις † εὐδαιμονίας ἔχει τὸ
	ἄκρον †
	 cf.  96b, schol. Pind.  5. 1a (ii 172 Drachm.), Plut.  5
	(vii 212 Bern.) + tr. Lat. ( 213)
	1 ἄνευ τὰς Neue ἄνευθ᾿ Ahrens
	2 om. schol. [c dimgray]Pyth.[/c], Sa. abiudic. Ahrens, alii Volper: δ᾿
	ἐξ ἀμφ. codd. ἔχει ἄκρον Plut.

	149 Ap. Dysc. [c dimgray]Pron.[/c] 126b (i 99 Schneider)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	καὶ σὺν τῷ ῾α᾿ λέγεται (sc. σφι) παρ᾿ Αἰολεῦσιν·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ὄτα πάννυχος ἄσφι κατάγρει
	Σαπφώ.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	150 Max. Tyr. 18. 9 (p. 232 Hobein)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἀναίθεται (ὁ Σωκράτης) τῇ Ξανθίππῃ ὀδυρομένῃ ὅτε ἀπέθνῃσκεν, ἡ δὲ (sc. Σαπφὼ) τῇ θυγατρί·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	οὐ γὰρ θέμις ἐν μοισοπόλων † οἰκίᾳ †
	θρῆνον ἔμμεν᾿· οὔ κ᾿ ἄμμι πρέποι τάδε.
	1 δόμῳ Hartung
	2 τάδε πρέποι Lobel
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 160[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	148 Scholiast on Pindar (‘wealth adorned with virtues brings the opportunity for all
	sorts of things’)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The meaning is: wealth, when not alone but embellished by virtue, opportunely enjoys its own
	benefits and those of virtue and has a wise concern for the pursuit of what is good. For
	neither of these is welcome on its own. Cf. Callimachus . . . and Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Wealth without virtue is no harmless neighbour.
	The blending of both brings the height of happiness.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]

	149 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Pronouns[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And σφι, ‘to them’, is used in Aeolic with initial α
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]; cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	When night-long (sleep) closes their (eyes)

	150 Maximus of Tyre, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Socrates was angry with Xanthippe for lamenting when he was dying, and Sappho was angry with
	her daughter:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	For it is not right that there should be lamentation in the house of those who serve the
	Muses. That would not be fitting for us.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Text uncertain; second line perhaps not by S.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. Alc. 313.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Metre and therefore text uncertain.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	151
	[c dimgray]Etymologicum Genuinum[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἄωρος: a lengthened form of ὦρος, which has the same meaning, ‘sleep’: cf. Callimachus (fr.
	177. 28) and Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	and night’s black sleep (closes . . .) eyes

	152 Scholiast on Apollonius of Rhodes
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἐρευθήεσσα,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] ‘red’, is used
	instead of πυρρά, ‘flame-coloured’, or ὑπέρυθρος, ‘ruddy’. This is contrary to Sappho’s
	description:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	mixed with all kinds of colours.

	153 Atilius Fortunatianus (on the so-called choriambic dimeter catalectic in Horace,
	[c dimgray]Odes[/c] 1. 8 [c dimgray]Lydia dic per omnes[/c])
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	cf. Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]
	sweet-voiced girl
	[m1]\ [/m]

	154 Hephaestion, [c dimgray]Handbook on Metres[/c] (on the ionic [c dimgray]a maiore[/c])
	[m1]\ [/m]
	And there are brachycatalectic trimeters which are called Praxilleans; these have an ionic in
	the first metron and a trochaic in the second (– – ⏑ ⏑ /
	– ⏑ – ⏑ / – –). Compare this example from
	Sappho:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	The
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] moon was coming into view in its fulness, and
	when the women took their position round the altar . . .

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. Anacr. 381.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Probably the beginning of a poem.

	155 Max. Tyr. 18. 9d (p. 231 Hobein)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	νῦν μὲν ἐπιτιμᾷ ταύταις (sc. Γοργοῖ καὶ Ἀνδρομέδᾳ), νῦν δὲ ἐλέγχει, καὶ εἰρωνεύεται αὐτὰ
	ἐκεῖνα τὰ Σωκράτους· ‘τὸν Ἴωνα χαίρειν’ φησὶν ὁ Σωκράτης (Pl. [c dimgray]Ion[/c] 530a)·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	πόλλα μοι τὰν Πωλυανάκτιδα παῖδα χαίρην
	Σαπφὼ λέγει.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Knebel: τὸν codd.

	156 Demetr. [c dimgray]Eloc.[/c] 161s. (p. 37 Radermacher)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἐκ δὲ ὑπερβολῶν χάριτες μάλιστα αἱ ἐν ταῖς κωμῳδίαις, πᾶσα δὲ ὑπερβολὴ ἀδύνατος, ὡς
	Ἀριστοφάνης . . . τοῦ δὲ αὐτοῦ εἴδους καὶ τὰ τοιαῦτά ἐστιν, ὑγιέστερος κολοκύντης καὶ
	φαλακρότερος εὐδίας καὶ τὰ Σαπφικά·
	[m1]\ [/m]

	πόλυ πάκτιδος ἀδυμελεστέρα . . .
	χρύσω χρυσοτέρα . . .
	 cf. Demetr.  127 (p. 30 Rad.)
	τὸ δὲ ‘χρύσω χρυσοτέρα’ τὸ Σαπφικὸν ἐν ὑπερβολῇ λέγεται καὶ αὐτὸ καὶ ἀδυνάτως, πλὴν αὐτῷ γε
	τῷ ἀδυνάτῳ χάριν ἔχει, οὐ ψυχρότητα. ὃ δὴ καὶ μάλιστα θαυμάσειεν ἄν τις Σαπφοῦς τῆς θείας, ὅτι
	φύσει κινδυνώδει πράγματι καὶ δυσκατορθώτῳ ἐχρήσατο ἐπιχαρίτως.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Greg. Cor. in Hermog. [c dimgray]Meth. (Rhet. Gr.[/c] vii 1236 Walz)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	αἰσχρῶς μὲν κολακεύει τὴν ἀκοὴν ἐκεῖνα ὅσα εἰσὶν ἐρωτικά, οἷον τὰ Ἀνακρέοντος, τὰ Σαπφοῦς,
	οἷον γάλακτος λευκοτέρα, ὕδατος ἁπαλωτέρα, πηκτίδων ἐμμελεστέρα, ἵππου γαυροτέρα, ῥόδων
	ἁβροτέρα, ἱματίου ἑανοῦ μαλακωτέρα, χρυσοῦ τιμιωτέρα.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 164[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	155 Maximus of Tyre, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]

	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sometimes she censures them (Gorgo and Andromeda), at other times she cross-examines them, and
	she uses irony just like Socrates. For Socrates says, ‘Good-day to you, Ion’,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] and
	Sappho says:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	I wish the daughter of the house of Polyanax a very good day.

	156 Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c] (on hyperbole)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The charms of comedy in particular are due to hyperbole, and every hyperbole involves an
	impossibility; cf. Aristophanes . . . Of the same kind are such phrases as ‘healthier than a
	pumpkin’, ‘balder than a cloudless sky’, and Sappho’s:
	[m1]\ [/m]

	far more sweet-sounding than a lyre . . .
	more golden than gold
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] . . .
	Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho’s phrase, ‘more golden than gold’, is certainly expressed as a hyperbole and involves
	an impossibility, but it does not fall flat: rather it derives charm from the impossibility.
	Indeed one of the most amazing characteristics of the divine Sappho is that she uses with charm
	a device that is of itself hazardous and difficult.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Gregory of Corinth on Hermogenes
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The ear is basely flattered by erotic phrases such as those of Anacreon and Sappho; for
	example, ‘whiter than milk’, ‘more gentle than water’, ‘more tuneful than lyres’,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	‘haughtier than a mare’, ‘more delicate than roses’,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] ‘softer than a fine robe’,
	‘more precious than gold’.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]See test. 20, fr. 99.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Opening words of Plato’s [c dimgray]Ion[/c]; Socrates goes on to refute Ion.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Both perhaps comparisons of a girl.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Cf. 117A
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 165[/c]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	Alas for Adonis

	⏑ ⏑) and using the seven half-unit trochaic (– ⏑
	– –) instead of the six (– ⏑ –
	⏑), e.g.
	[m1]\ [/m]

-142.173 (SAPPHO, Fragments)

SAPPHO, Fragments
	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]

	δέδυκε μὲν ἀ σελάννα
	καὶ Πληΐαδες· μέσαι δὲ
	νύκτες, παρὰ δ᾿ ἔρχετ᾿ ὤρα,
	ἔγω δὲ μόνα κατεύδω.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. schol. A in Heph. 11 (p. 147 Consbr.), Arsen. 18. 51 = Apostol. 5. 98c (ii 363
	Leutsch–Schn.) (Σαπφοῦς)

	168C (Voigt) (= fr. adesp. 964 [c dimgray]P.M.G.[/c]) Demetr. [c dimgray]Eloc.[/c] 164 (p. 37
	Radermacher)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τὸ μὲν γὰρ εὔχαρι μετὰ κόσμου ἐκφέρεται καὶ δι᾿ ὀνομάτων καλῶν, ἃ μάλιστα ποιεῖ τὰς χάριτας, οἶον τὸ
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ποικίλλεται μὲν
	γαῖα πολυστέφανος
	 Sa. ded. Wilamowitz

	169 Schol. [c dimgray]Il.[/c] 14. 241 (ἐπισχοίης) (ii 46 Dindorf)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τῷ δὲ χαρακτῆρι γενόμενον ὅμοιον τῷ ἰοίην καὶ
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀγαγοίην
	παρὰ Σαπφοῖ . . . εἰκότως ἐβαρυτονήθη τὸ ἐπισχοίης.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	169Α (Voigt) Hsch. A 1621
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἀθρήματα· δῶρα πεμπόμενα παρὰ τῶν συγγενῶν ταῖς γαμουμέναις παρθένοις παρὰ Λεσβίοις.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Sa. ded. Edmonds ad fr. 44. 9
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 172[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	The moon has set and the Pleiades; it is midnight, and time goes by, and I lie
	alone.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	168C (Voigt) Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Charm is produced in company with ornamentation and by the use of beautiful words, which
	contribute most to it; e.g.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	the earth with its many garlands is embroidered . . .
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	169 Scholiast on [c dimgray]Iliad[/c] (on the optative ἐπισχοίης)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	This is similar in type to the forms ἰοίην, (‘I might go’) (182) and ἀγαγοίην,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	I might lead,
	in Sappho, and so it is reasonably accented on the penultimate
	syllable.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	169A (Voigt) Hesychius, [c dimgray]Lexicon[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἀθρήματα: Lesbian term for presents sent from relatives to girls who are about to be
	married.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Attributed to Sappho by Arsenius ([c dimgray]c.[/c] 1500); ascription rejected by
	Wilamowitz, Lobel, Page.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Attributed to Sappho by Wilamowitz; Lobel and Page dissent.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]I.e. the reading ἐπίσχοιες is wrong; see Chantraine, [c dimgray]Gramm. Hom[/c]. i
	464.

-142.175 (SAPPHO, Fragments)
%Greek Lyric Sappho
SAPPHO, Fragments
	[c darkmagenta][b]SAPPHO, Fragments[/b][/c]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]

	170 Strab. 13. 1. 68 (iii 62 Kramer)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Κάναι δὲ πολίχνιον Λοκρῶν τῶν ἐκ Κύνου κατὰ τὰ ἄκρα τῆς Λέσβου τὰ νοτιώτατα κείμενον ἐν τῇ
	Καναίᾳ· αὕτη δὲ μέχρι τῶν Ἀργινουσσῶν διήκει καὶ τῆς ὑπερκειμένης ἄκρας, ἣν Αἶγά τινες
	ὀνομάζουσιν ὁμωνύμως τῷ ζώῳ· δεῖ δὲ μακρῶς τὴν δεύτεραν συλλαβὴν ἐκφέρειν Αἰγᾶν, ὡς Ἀκτᾶν καὶ
	Ἀρχᾶν· οὕτω γὰρ καὶ τὸ ὄρος ὅλον ὠνομάζετο, ὃ νῦν Κάνην καὶ Κάνας λέγουσι . . . ὕστερον δὲ αὐτὸ
	τὸ ἀκρωτήριον Αἰγᾶ † κεκλῆσθαι †, ὡς Σαπφώ, τὸ δὲ λοιπὸν Κάνη καὶ Κάναι.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	171 Phot. (p. 57 Reitzenstein) = [c dimgray]Anecd. Gr.[/c] i 370 Bekker
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἄκακος·
	ὁ κακοῦ μὴ πεπειραμένος, οὐχ ὁ χρηστοήθης. οὕτω Σαπφώ.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	172 Max. Tyr. 18. 9gh (p. 232 Hobein)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἡ Διοτίμα λέγει ὅτι θάλλει μὲν Ἔρως εὐπορῶν, ἀποθνήσκει δὲ ἀπορῶν· τοῦτο ἐκείνη (sc. Σαπφὼ)
	ξυλλαβοῦσα εἶπεν γλυκύπικρον (fr. 130. 2) καὶ
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ἀλγεσίδωρον.

	173 Choerob. in Theodos. (i 331 Hilgard)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἀμάμαξυς ἀμαμάξυος (σημαίνει δὲ ἄμπελον ἀναδενδράδα), τὸ γὰρ παρὰ Σαπφοῖ ἀμαμάξυδος παράλογόν
	ἐστιν.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf.  (p. 14 Calame) =  77. 7 (Σαπφὼ δὲ . . . ἀμαμαξύδες λέγει),
	A 1482

	174 Orion (3. 12ss. Sturz)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	\[ἀμάρα\] οὕτως ἐν ὑπομνήματι Σαπφοῦς.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 174[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	170 Strabo, [c dimgray]Geography[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Canae is a tiny town of the Locrians from Cynus and lies
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] opposite the southernmost
	points of Lesbos; it is situated in the Canaean region, which extends to the islands of
	Arginusae and the promontory above them, which some people call Αἴς (accus. Αἶγα, Aega), ‘the
	goat’, with the same name as the animal; but the second syllable should be lengthened, Aegā,
	like Actā and Archā, for this used to be the name of the whole mountain which they now call Cane
	or Canae . . . ; later the promontory itself was called Aega, as in Sappho,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] and the
	remainder Cane or Canae.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	171 Photius, [c dimgray]Lexicon[/c] (on ἄκακος, ‘free from evil’)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	innocent,
	used of one who has had no experience of evil, not of one who is virtuous; so Sappho.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	172 Maximus of Tyre, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Diotima says
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] that Love flourishes when he has abundance but dies when he is in
	need: Sappho combined these ideas and called Love bitter-sweet (130. 2) and
	[m1]\ [/m]

	pain-giver.

	173 Choeroboscus on Theodosius (on feminine nouns in -υς)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἀμάμαξυς, genitive ἀμαμάξυος, meaning a vine which grows up trees: the genitive ἀμαμάξυδος in
	Sappho is irregular.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	174 Orion, [c dimgray]Lexicon[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	\[ἀμάρα, ‘channel’\]:
	so in a commentary
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] on Sappho.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]On the mainland of Asia Minor.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]‘As in Sappho’ may be a marginal gloss.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]See Plato, [c dimgray]Symposium[/c] 203b ff.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Perhaps Chamaeleon’s: see testt. 1, 8.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 175[/c]

	178 Zenob. 3. 3 (i 58 Leutsch–Schneidewin)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Γέλλως παιδοφιλωτέρα,
	ἐπὶ τῶν ἀώρως τελευτησάντων, ἤτοι ἐπὶ τῶν φιλοτέκνων μέν, τροφῇ δὲ
	διαφθειρόντων αὐτά. Γελλὼ γάρ τις ἦν παρθένος, καὶ ἐπειδὴ ἀώρως ἐτελεύτησε, φασὶν οἱ Λέσβιοι
	αὐτῆς τὸ φάντασμα ἐπιφοιτᾶν ἐπὶ τὰ παιδία, καὶ τοὺς τῶν ἀώρων θανάτους αὐτῇ ἀνατιθέασι. μέμνηται
	ταύτης Σαπφώ.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf.  (p. 45 Calame) =  795. 6ss.,  Γ 112, Hsch. Γ

	179 Phrynich. [c dimgray]Praep. Sophist.[/c] (p. 60 von Borries)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Σαπφὼ δὲ γρύτην καλεῖ τὴν μύρων καὶ γυναικείων τινῶν θήκην.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 176[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	175 Apollonius Dyscolus, [c dimgray]Adverbs[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	As metaplasms
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] occur in nouns, e.g. ἐρυσάρματες, ‘drawing chariots’, λῖτα, ‘linen
	cloth’, and in Sappho αὔα,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	dawn . . .

	176 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	For, says Euphorion,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] the barōmos
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] and the barbitos, which Sappho and
	Anacreon (472) mention, as well as the magadis, the trigōnon and the sambūca, are all ancient
	instruments.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	177 Pollux, [c dimgray]Vocabulary[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The word βεῦδος,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	shift,
	found in Sappho, is the same as κιμβερικόν, which is a short
	diaphanous dress.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	178 Zenobius, [c dimgray]Proverbs[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Fonder of children than Gello
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c];
	a saying used of those who died prematurely,
	or of those who are fond of children but ruin them by their upbringing. For Gello was a girl,
	and since she died prematurely the Lesbians say her ghost haunts little children, and they
	attribute premature deaths to her. Sappho mentions her.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	179 Phrynichus, [c dimgray]Sophistic Preparation[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho calls a receptacle for perfumes and other feminine accoutrements γρύτα,
	[m1]\ [/m]
	vanity-bag
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Forms derived from a non-existent nom. sing.; but the [c dimgray]Etymologica[/c] imply
	that αὔα is nominative (fem. sing.).
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]3rd c. b.c. epic poet and scholar.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Also ‘barmos’, used by Alc. 70. 4.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c]All types of lyre or harp.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Perhaps used of a rapacious rival.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 177[/c]

	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	Aristaenetus, [c dimgray]Love-letters[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	μελλιχόφωνοι,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	soft-voiced:
	this is Sappho’s most delightful word.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	186 John of Alexandria, [c dimgray]Rules of Accentuation[/c] (on the rule that the acute accent
	never falls on the fourth-last syllable)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	I exclude the form Μήδεϊα,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	Medea,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	in Sappho, because she has resolved the diphthong ει.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	187
	[c dimgray]Homeric Parsings[/c] (on [c dimgray]Il[/c]. 2. 761 Μοῦσα, ‘Muse’)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The genitive plural of Μοῦσα is Μωσάων in Laconian and
	Μοισάων,
	[m1]\ [/m]

	of the Muses,
	in Sappho.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	188 Maximus of Tyre, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Socrates calls Love a sophist, Sappho
	[m1]\ [/m]

	tale-weaver.

	189 Phrynichus, [c dimgray]Attic Words and Phrases[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	νίτρον
	[m1]\ [/m]

	soda:

	this is what an Aeolian would call it, e.g. Sappho, with the ν; but an
	Athenian would pronounce it with the λ, λίτρον.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]S. may have used both adjectives; for the second see 71. 6.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]But the word may be the fem, of μήδεις, ‘no-one’; so Voigt.

	190 Scholiast on the [c dimgray]Iliad[/c] (on ἄϊδρις, ‘unknowledgeable’)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	very knowledgeable,
	is wrong, unless of course its declension was the same as in Attic;
	for Sophocles used the form ἴδριδα . . .

	191 Pollux, [c dimgray]Vocabulary[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	5) and Alcaeus (362. 1). These two also mention celery.

	[m1]\ [/m]

	192 Pollux, [c dimgray]Vocabulary[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	their material; cf. Sappho’s
	[m1]\ [/m]

	gold-knobbed goblets.

	193 Aristides, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]

	fortunate and saying that the Muses had made her truly blessed and enviable, and that she would
	not be forgotten even when she was dead.

	[m1]\ [/m]

	194 Himerius, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]

	lyre and form into the epithalamium. After the contests
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] she goes into the bridal
	chamber, garlands the room and makes up the bed, then she (gathers?)

	†γράφει† παρθένους <εἰς> νυμφεῖον, ἄγει
	καὶ Ἀφροδίτην ἐφ᾿ ἅρματι Χαρίτων καὶ χορὸν Ἐρώτων συμπαίστορα· καὶ τῆς μὲν ὑακίνθῳ τὰς κόμας
	σφίγξασα, πλὴν ὅσαι μετώπῳ μερίζονται, τὰς λοιπὰς ταῖς αὔραις ἀφῆκεν ὑποκυμαίνειν εἰ πλήττοιεν·
	τῶν δὲ τὰ πτερὰ καὶ τοὺς βοστρύχους χρυσῷ κοσμήσασα πρὸ τοῦ δίφρου σπεύδει πομπεύοντας καὶ δᾷδα
	κινοῦντας μετάρσιον.

	195 Demetr. [c dimgray]Eloc.[/c] 166 (p. 37 Radermacher)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	καὶ περὶ ἀλκύονος, καὶ ἅπαν καλὸν ὄνομα ἐνύφανται αὐτῆς τῇ ποιήσει, τὰ αὐτὴ εἰργάσατο.

	αὖξον καὶ τρέφον καὶ ἄρδον ἅμα εὐθυμίᾳ, ὑακινθίνῳ μὲν ἄνθει οὐδαμῶς ὅμοιον ([c dimgray]Od.[/c] 6. 231),
	ἀλλ᾿ οἷον οὐδὲν πώποτε γῆ καὶ ἥλιος ἀνθρώποις ἔφηναν.

	198 Schol. Ap. Rhod. 3. 26 (p. 216 Wendel)

	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Schol. Theoer. 13. 1–2c (p. 258 Wendel)
	Ἀλκαῖος (τὸν Ἔρωτα εἶπεν) Ἴριδος καὶ Ζεφύρου (fr. 327), Σαπφὼ Ἀφροδίτης καὶ Οὐρανοῦ.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Paus. 9. 27. 3 (iii 58 Spiro)
	Σαπφὼ δὲ ἡ Λεσβία πολλά τε καὶ οὐχ ὁμολογοῦντα ἀλλήλοις ἐς Ἔρωτα ᾖσε.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 184[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	the girls into the bridal room and brings in Aphrodite herself on the Graces’ chariot with her chorus of
	Loves to join in the fun. She binds Aphrodite’s hair in hyacinth except for what is parted by
	her brow; this she lets ripple in whatever breezes may blow. She adorns the Loves’ wings and
	tresses with gold and urges them on in procession before the chariot, waving their torches in
	the air.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	195 Demetrius, [c dimgray]On Style[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	This is why when Sappho sings of beauty her words are beautiful and sweet; so too when she
	sings of loves and spring and the halycon: every type of beautiful word is woven into her
	poetry, and some of them are her own creation.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	196Aelius Aristides, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . the brilliance which lies over all the city,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] not destroying the eyes, as
	Sappho said,
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] but strengthening, nourishing and also watering it with gladness, not
	at all ‘like the hyacinth flower’
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] but unlike anything that earth and sun ever
	revealed to man.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	197 Libanius, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	So if nothing prevented the Lesbian Sappho from praying that the night be made twice as long
	for her, let it be possible for me too to make a similar request.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	198 Scholiast on Apollonius of Rhodes
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho makes Eros child of Earth and Heaven. Scholiast on Theocritus
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Alcaeus (327) said Eros was the child of Iris and Zephyr; Sappho made him the child of
	Aphrodite and Heaven. Pausanias, [c dimgray]Description of Greece[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The Lesbian Sappho made many inconsistent references to Eros in her poems.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	χρημάτων μεγάλων ὑπὸ ἀνδρὸς Μυτιληναίου Χαράξου τοῦ Σκαμανδρωνύμου παιδός, ἀδελφεοῦ δὲ Σαπφοῦς
	τῆς μουσοποιοῦ . . . Χάραξος δὲ ὡς λυσάμενος Ῥοδῶπιν ἀπενόστησε ἐς Μυτιλήνην, ἐν μέλεϊ Σαπφὼ

	Δωρίχαν, ἐρωμένην τοῦ ἀδελφοῦ αὐτῆς Χαράξου γεγονυῖαν οἶνον κατάγοντος εἰς Ναύκρατιν Λέσβιον

	199 Scholiast on Apollonius of Rhodes
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The story goes that Selene comes down to this cave
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] to meet Endymion. Sappho and
	Nicander in [c dimgray]Europia[/c] Book 2 tell the story of the love of Selene (the Moon).

	200Scholiast on Hesiod, [c dimgray]Works and Days[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho says Peitho, ‘Persuasion’, was the daughter of Aphrodite.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	201 Aristotle, [c dimgray]Rhetoric[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho says that death is an evil: the gods have so decided, otherwise they would die.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	202 Herodotus, [c dimgray]Histories[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Rhodopis
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] was brought to Egypt by the Samian Xanthes; she came to ply her trade,
	but she was freed for a great sum of money by a Mytilenaean, Charaxus, the son of
	Scamandronymus and the brother of the poetess Sappho . . . After freeing Rhodopis Charaxus
	returned to Mytilene, where he was roundly abused by Sappho in one of her
	songs.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Strabo, [c dimgray]Geography[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	It
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] is called the tomb of the prostitute, and it was built by her lovers; the
	lyric poetess Sappho calls the woman Doricha, and she was the mistress of Sappho’s brother
	Charaxus, who imported Lesbian wine to Naucratis; other writers call her
	Rhodopis.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	334 says Charaxus had children by Rhodopis; this may account in part for S.’s
	hostility.

	Athen. 13. 596bc (iii 314 Kaibel)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ἐνδόξους δὲ ἑταίρας . . . ἤνεγκεν καὶ ἡ Ναύκρατις· Δωρίχαν τε, ἣν ἡ καλὴ Σαπφὼ ἐρωμένην
	γενομένην Χαράξου τοῦ ἀδελφοῦ αὐτῆς κατ᾿ ἐμπορίαν εἰς τὴν Ναύκρατιν ἀπαίροντος διὰ τῆς ποιήσεως
	διαβάλλει ὡς πολλὰ τοῦ Χαράξου νοσφισαμένην.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Phot. s.vv. Ῥοδώπιδος ἀνάθημα (p. 490 Reitzenstein) =  P211 (iv 297 Adler)
	. . . ἦν δὲ Θρᾷσσα τὸ γένος, ἐδούλευσε δὲ σὺν Αἰσώπῳ Ἰάδμονι Μυτιληναίῳ, ἐλυτρώσατο δ᾿ αὐτὴν
	Χάραξος ὁ Σαπφοῦς ἀδελφός· ἡ δὲ Σαπφὼ Δωρίχαν αὐτὴν καλεῖ.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 App. Prov. 4. 51 (i 445 Leutsch—Schneidewin)
	ἡ Ῥοδῶπις ἑταίρα ἧν περὶ Ναύκρατιν τῆς Αἰγύπτου, ἧς καὶ Σαπφὼ μνημονεύει καὶ Ἡρόδοτος.

	 P. Oxy. 1800, fr. 1. 7–13: v. test. 1

	203 Athen. 10. 425a (ii 424 Kaibel)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Σαπφώ τε ἡ καλὴ πολλαχοῦ Λάριχον τὸν ἀδελφὸν ἐπαινεῖ ὡς οἰνοχοοῦντα ἐν τῷ πρυτανείῳ τοῖς

	204 Schol. Pind. [c dimgray]Pyth.[/c] 4. 410c (ii 153 Drachmann)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	ὁ δὲ χρυσὸς ἄφθαρτος. καὶ ἡ Σαπφὼ < > ὅτι Διὸς παῖς ὁ χρυσὸς. . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 Paus. 8. 18. 5 (ii 301 Spiro)
	καίτοι καθαρεύειν γε τὸν χρυσὸν ἀπὸ τοῦ ἰοῦ ἤ τε ποιητρία μάρτυς ἐστὶν ἡ Λεσβία καὶ αὐτὸς ὁ
