	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 188[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]
	Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Naucratis too attracted famous prostitutes, among them Dorieha; she became the mistress of
	Sappho’s brother, Charaxus, a trader at Naucratis, and the lovely Sappho attacks her in her
	poems on the grounds that she robbed him of large sums.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Photius and [c dimgray]Suda[/c] on Rhodopis’ offering
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Rhodopis was Thracian by birth; along with Aesop she was the slave of the Mytilenaean Iadmon;
	she was ransomed by Charaxus, the brother of Sappho; Sappho calls her Doricha.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[c dimgray]Appendix to the proverbs[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Rhodopis was a prostitute in the area of Naucratis in Egypt; she is mentioned by both Sappho
	and Herodotus.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	203 Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The lovely Sappho often praises her brother Larichus
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] because he poured the wine
	for the Mytilenaeans in the town-hall.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Scholiast on the [c dimgray]Iliad[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	For it was the custom, as Sappho says, for handsome young noblemen to pour the wine.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	204 Scholiast on Pindar (‘the imperishable coverlet’, i.e. the golden fleece)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Gold is indestructible: Sappho says
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] . . . (and Pindar
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] says) that gold
	is the child of Zeus . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Pausanias, [c dimgray]Description of Greece[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	That gold is not corrupted by rust is attested by the Lesbian poetess
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] and proved
	by the metal itself.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Test. 15 follows.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. testt. 1, 2, 14.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]S.’s words about gold are lost, but Pausanias presumably refers to the
	passage.
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]Fr. 222 Snell.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]So in Theognis 450 ff.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 189[/c]

	205 Aul. Gell. [c dimgray]Noct. Att.[/c] 20. 7 (ii 301 Hosius)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Homerus ([c dimgray]Il.[/c] 24. 602) pueros puellasque eius (sc. Niobae) bis senos dicit fuisse,
	Euripides (fr. 455 N[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]) bis septenos, Sappho bis novenos, Bacchylides (fr. 20D. 4ss.
	Snell) et Pindarus (fr. 65 Snell) bis denos, quidam alii scriptores tres fuisse solos
	dixerunt.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. Aelian.  12. 36 (p. 141 Dilts)

	206 Serv. in Verg. [c dimgray]Aen.[/c] 6. 21 (ii 9 Thilo–Hagen)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	quidam septem pueros et septem puellas accipi volunt, et Plato dicit in Phaedone (58a) et
	Sappho in lyricis et Bacchylides in dithyrambis (17. 1ss.) et Euripides in Hercule ([c dimgray]H. F.[/c]
	1326), quos liberavit secum Theseus.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	207 Serv. in Verg. [c dimgray]Ecl.[/c] 6. 42 (iii 72 Thilo–Hagen)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Prometheus . . . post factos a se homines dicitur . . . ignem furatus, quem hominibus
	indicavit. ob quam causam irati dii duo mala immiserunt terris, mulieres et morbos, sicut et
	Sappho et Hesiodus ([c dimgray]Theog.[/c] 570ss., [c dimgray]Op.[/c] 70ss., 100ss.) memorant.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	208 Himer. [c dimgray]Or.[/c] 46. 6 (p. 186s. Colonna)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τὰ δὲ σὰ νῦν δέον καὶ αὐτῷ τῷ Μουσαγέτῃ εἰκάζεσθαι, οἷον αὐτὸν καὶ Σαπφὼ καὶ Πίνδαρος (cf.
	[c dimgray]Pae.[/c] 3) ἐν ᾠδῇ κόμῃ τε χρυσῇ καὶ λύραις κοσμήσαντες, κύκνοις ἔποχον εἰς Ἑλικῶνα
	πέμπουσι, Μούσαις Χάρισί τε ὁμοῦ συγχορεύσοντα.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	209 Eust. [c dimgray]Epist.[/c] 42 ([c dimgray]Opusc[/c]. p. 345 Tafel)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	φιλία τις δηλαδὴ πολυρέμβαστος καὶ καλὸν δοκοῦσα εἴποι ἂν ἡ Σαπφὼ δημόσιον.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 190[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	205 Aulus Gellius, [c dimgray]Attic Nights[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Homer says Niobe had six sons and six daughters, Euripides says seven of each, Sappho
	nine,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] Bacchylides and Pindar ten, certain other writers only three of each.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	206 Servius on Virgil (‘seven victims of the Minotaur each year’)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Some think seven boys and seven girls are meant, as Plato has it in the [c dimgray]Phaedo[/c] (58a),
	Sappho in her lyric poems, Bacchylides in his dithyrambs (17. 1 ff.) and Euripides in the
	[c dimgray]Hercules[/c] (1326). Theseus freed them along with himself.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	207 Servius on Virgil
	[m1]\ [/m]
	After creating men Prometheus is said to have stolen fire and revealed it to men. The gods
	were angered by this and sent two evils on the earth, women and disease; such is the account
	given by Sappho and Hesiod ([c dimgray]Theogony[/c] 570 ff., [c dimgray]Works and Days[/c] 70 ff., 100 ff.)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	208 Himerius, [c dimgray]Orations[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Your situation should now be compared with that of the Leader of the Muses
	himself
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] as he appears when Sappho and Pindar ([c dimgray]Paean[/c] 3) in their songs deck
	him out with golden hair and lyre and send him drawn by swans to Mount Helicon to dance there
	with the Muses and Graces.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	209 Eustathius, [c dimgray]Letters[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . a friendship which is gadabout and, as Sappho would say,
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] makes a virtue of
	publicity.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Cf. 142.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Apollo; cf. Alc. 307(c).
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Perhaps with reference to Charaxus and Rhodopis (Doricha).
	[m1]\ [/m]

-142.193 (SAPPHO, Fragments)

SAPPHO, Fragments

	210 Phot. 81. 12s. (p. 274 Naber)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	θάψος· ξύλον ᾦ ξανθίζουσι τὰ ἔρια καὶ τὰς τρίχας· ὃ Σαπφὼ Σκυθικὸν ξύλον λέγει.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	 cf. schol. Theoc. 2. 88 (p. 286 Wendel) (a) σκυθάριον, ὥς φησι Σαπφώ, (b) Σκυθικὸν ξύλον, ὥς
	φησι καὶ Σαπφώ, Hsch. Θ 155, Σ 1155, 1156

	211(a) Ps.-Palaeph. [c dimgray]de incred.[/c] 48 ([c dimgray]Myth. Gr.[/c] iii (2) 69 Festa)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τῷ Φάωνι βίος ἦν περὶ πλοῖον εἶναι καὶ θάλασσαν· πορθμὸς ἦν ἡ θάλασσα· ἔγκλημα δὲ οὐδὲν παρ᾿
	οὐδενὸς ἐκομίζετο, ἐπεὶ καὶ μέτριος ἦν καὶ παρὰ τῶν ἐχόντων μόνον ἐδέχετο. θαῦμα ἧν τοῦ τροποῦ
	παρὰ τοῖς Λεσβίοις. ἐπαινεῖ τὸν ἄνθρωπον ἡ θεός· Ἀφροδίτην λέγουσι τὴν θεόν· καὶ ὑποδῦσα θέαν
	ἀνθρώπου γυναικὸς ἤδη γεγηρακυίας, τῷ Φάωνι διαλέγεται περὶ πλοῦ. τάχυς ἦν ἐκεῖνος καὶ
	διακομίσαι καὶ μηδὲν ἀπαιτῆσαι. τί οὖν ἐπὶ τούτοις ἡ θεός; ἀμεῖψαί φασι τὸν ἄνθρωπον, καὶ
	ἀμείβεται νεότητι καὶ κάλλει τὸν γέροντα. οὗτος ὁ Φάων ἐστίν, ἐφ᾿ ᾧ τὸν ἔρωτα αὐτῆς ἡ Σαπφὼ
	πολλάκις ἐμελοποίησεν.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	cf. testt. 3, 5, 38, Ov. [c dimgray]Her.[/c] 15, Porphyr. et ‘Acron.’ in Hor. [c dimgray]Carm.[/c] 2. 13. 25,
	Apostol. 17. 80 = Arsen. 52. 16 (ii 707 Leutsch–Schn.), Alciphr. [c dimgray]Ep.[/c] 1. 11. 4 (p.
	13s. Schepers), Plut. [c dimgray]Prov.[/c] 1. 29 (i 325 Leutsch–Schn.), Serv. in Verg.
	[c dimgray]Aen.[/c] 3. 279 (i 390 Thilo–H.), Luc. [c dimgray]D. Mort.[/c] 9. 2, schol. Luc. [c dimgray]D.
	Meretr.[/c] 12. 1, schol. Berol. in Lib. [c dimgray]Ep.[/c] 257 = [c dimgray]Sud.[/c] Φ 89 = Phot. s.v. Φάων =
	[c dimgray]Et. Gen.[/c] (p. 45 Calame)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(b)(i) Aelian. [c dimgray]V. H.[/c] 12. 18 (p. 135 Dilts)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	τὸν Φάωνα κάλλιστον ὄντα ἀνθρώπων ἡ Ἀφροδίτη ἐν θριδακίναις ἔκρυψε.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(ii) Athen. 2. 69cd (i 163 Kaibel)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	καὶ Καλλίμαχος δέ φησιν (fr. 478 Pf.) ὅτι ἡ Ἀφροδίτη τὸν Ἄδωνιν ἐν θριδακίνῃ κρύψειεν . . .
	καὶ Εὔβουλος δ᾿ ἐν Ἀστύτοις φησί (fr. 14 Kock)· . . . ἐν τῷ λαχάνῳ τούτῳ γάρ, ὡς λόγος, ποτὲ |
	τὸν Ἄδωνιν ἀποθανόντα προὔθηκεν Κύπρις· . . . Κρατῖνος δέ φησι (fr. 330 Kock) Φάωνος ἐρασθεῖσαν
	τὴν Ἀφροδίτην ἐν ῾καλαῖς θριδακίναις᾿ αὐτὸν ἀποκρύψαι, Μαρσύας δ᾿ ὁ νεώτερος ἐν χλόῃ κριθῶν.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 192[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c darkmagenta][b]Sappho[/b][/c]

	210 Photius, [c dimgray]Lexicon[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	θάψος, ‘fustic’: a wood with which they dye wool and hair yellow; Sappho calls it Scythian
	wood.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	211(a) Pseudo-Palaephatus, [c dimgray]On Incredible Events[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Phaon’s life revolved round his boat and the sea or, rather, a strait. He met with no
	complaint from anyone, since he was a fair man and accepted money only from those who were
	rich. There was amazement among the Lesbians at his way of life. The goddess, by whom they mean
	Aphrodite, approved of this man, so she assumed the appearance of a mortal, an aged woman, and
	spoke with Phaon about a crossing. He quickly carried her over and asked for nothing. What did
	the goddess do then? They say she transformed the old man and repaid him with youth and beauty.
	This then is the Phaon about her love for whom Sappho often sang in her lyric
	poetry.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(b)(i) Aelian, [c dimgray]Historical Miscellanies[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Phaon, the most beautiful of mortals, was hidden by Aphrodite among lettuces.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(ii) Athenaeus, [c dimgray]Scholars at Dinner[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Callimachus says that Aphrodite hid Adonis in a lettuce-bed . . .; Eubulus in [c dimgray]The Impotent
	Men[/c] says: ‘. . . for it was among these vegetables, the story goes, that the Cyprian once
	laid out the dead Adonis for burial . . .’; Cratinus says that when Aphrodite fell in love with
	Phaon she hid him among ‘fair lettuces’; the younger Marsyas says it was among green
	barley.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]
	[b][c dimgray]Footnotes[/c][/b]
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Acc. to the scholiast on Libanius, followed by the lexicographers, this Sappho
	was ‘not the poetess but a woman of Lesbos’; see testt. 3, 4 n. 2.
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]Note the identification or confusion of Phaon with Adonis.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	[c dimgray]Page number 193[/c]

	(iii) Comes Natalis, [c dimgray]Mythology[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho has left a written record that the dead Adonis was laid out among lettuces by
	Venus.
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(c) Pliny, [c dimgray]Natural History[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	The story about the erynge (‘sea-holly’) is remarkable: its root, they say, takes the shape
	of either the male or the female sex-organ; it is rarely so found, but if men find the male
	shape, they become sexually attractive; it was this, they say, that made Sappho fall in love
	with Phaon of Lesbos.

	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c]’: σύνδυγος,
	‘yoke-mate’, is used instead of σύνζυξ: Pleistodica will be called yoke-mate to Gorgo along
	with Gongyla.
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] For the name that has been given (i.e. Archeanassa?) is her usual one
	or one derived from (her family?) . . . Pleistodica . . . will be called (by her correct name?)

	213A (Voigt) = S 273–278 ([c dimgray]S. L. G.[/c]) Commentary on lyric poets (papyrus,
	1st c. or early 2nd c. a.d.)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(a) . . . Gongyla
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	Polyanax?) . . .

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	[m1]\ [/m]

	 . . . prosperity .
	. . Sappho . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	‘wicked . . . prayer . . . black . . . scarcely . . . how does this concern me? . . . grips

%Greek Lyric Sappho
SAPPHO, Fragments

	[c darkmagenta][b]Greek Lyric[/b][/c]
	(h)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	fr. 48 col. iii 36ss.: v. test. 14
	[m1]\ [/m]
	(i) fr. 59 1 \] . . αφ\[ 7 \].ουδια\[
	[m1]\ [/m]

	papyrus)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	and Sappho . . . Calliope

	[m1]\ [/m]

	214B Commentary on Sappho
	[m1]\ [/m]
	fr. 1 . . . (he?) . . . victors . . . (wars?)
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]; but she in peace and quiet teaching
	the noblest girls not only from the local families but also from families in Ionia
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c];
	and so highly esteemed among the citizens that Callias of Mytilene
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] said in . . .
	Aphrodite . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	fr. 2 col. i . . . (fate?) . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	‘. . . of the Muses . . .’
	as far as
	[m1]\ [/m]

	‘. . . from him . . . fated.’
	So she represents the women who frequent the (royal?) house
	and set great store on (excelling? quarreling?) and . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]
	col. ii . . . (Cleanactid? Archeanactid? Polyanactid?)
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] . . . Cleanactid . . . the
	daughter
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] . . . and reproached . . . nobility . . .
	[m1]\ [/m]

	157D.
	[c dimgray]Anth. Pal.[/c] 6. 269
	[m1]\ [/m]

	ὡς Σαπφοῦς·
	παῖδες, ἄφωνος ἐοῖσα ποτεννέπω αἴ τις ἔρηται,
	φωνὰν ἀκαμάταν κατθεμένα πρὸ ποδῶν·
	Αἰθοπίᾳ με κόρᾳ Λατοῦς ἀνέθηκεν Ἀρίστα
	ἁ Ἑρμοκλείδα τῶ Σαϋναϊάδα,
	[c dimgray]5[/c]σὰ πρόπολος, δέσποινα γυναικῶν· ᾇ σὺ χαρεῖσα
	πρόφρων ἁμετέραν εὐκλέισον γενεάν.

	[m1]\ [/m]

	158D.
	[c dimgray]Anth. Pal[/c]. 7. 489 (Plan.)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Σαπφοῦς·
	Τιμάδος ἅδε κόνις, τὰν δὴ πρὸ γάμοιο θανοῦσαν
	δέξατο Φερσεφόνας κυάνεος θάλαμος·
	ἇς καὶ ἀποφθιμένας πᾶσαι νεοθᾶγι σιδάρῳ
	ἅλικες ἱμερτὰν κρατὸς ἔθεντο κόμαν.

	159D.
	[c dimgray]Anth. Pal.[/c] 7. 505 (Plan.)
	[m1]\ [/m]

	τῷ γριπεῖ Πελάγωνι πατὴρ ἐπέθηκε Μενίσκος
	κύρτον καὶ κώπαν, μνᾶμα κακοζοΐας.
	1 ἀνέθηκε Plan.

	214C Cologne papyrus (2nd c. a.d.)
	[m1]\ [/m]
	. . . proud
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c] (Atthis)
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] . . . charming . . . with . . . mind . . .

	[m1]\ [/m]
	Epigrams

	157D. [c dimgray]Palatine Anthology[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Ascribed to Sappho
	[c dimgray][sup]1[/sup][/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Children, although I am voiceless I answer, if anyone ask, since I have had a tireless
	voice
	[c dimgray][sup]2[/sup][/c] set at my feet: ‘Arista, daughter of Hermoclides son of Saunaiadas, dedicated
	me to Aethopia,
	[c dimgray][sup]3[/sup][/c] daughter of Leto. She is your attendant, queen of women: rejoice in
	her
	[c dimgray][sup]4[/sup][/c] and graciously honour our family.’
	[m1]\ [/m]

	158D. [c dimgray]Palatine Anthology[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho
	[m1]\ [/m]
	This is the dust of Timas. She died before her marriage and was received by the dark chamber
	of Persephone. On her death all her companions took the lovely hair from their heads with
	newly-sharpened steel.
	[m1]\ [/m]

	159D. [c dimgray]Palatine Anthology[/c]
	[m1]\ [/m]
	Sappho
	[m1]\ [/m]
	On the tomb of Pelagon the fisherman Meniscus, his father, placed his basket and his oar,
	memorials of his sorry life.
	[m1]\ [/m]
